Mentioning Boundaries in Real Estate: Four or Name for Famous Properties
Defines boundaries and disputes necessity of four vs name for famous properties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi requirement for mentioning boundaries in a real estate claim and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on when the property name suffices.

Core Idea
Boundaries identify real estate; for famous properties, name may suffice per Sahibayn, but Abu Hanifa requires all four boundaries absolutely.

When claiming ownership of real estate, the plaintiff must describe the property so the court can identify it. The Hanafi school discusses whether all four boundaries must be stated or if a famous name suffices. This lesson examines the definitions of valid boundaries, the general rule, and the difference of opinion between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of boundaries
The text defines 'hudud' as features that distinguish real estate, like houses and lands. Market, road, and river are invalid because they change over time.
2
Identify the general requirement
The basic rule is that the plaintiff must mention all four boundaries (east, west, north, south) of the claimed property. This is wajib.
Zahir al-riwayah supports this absolute requirement.
3
Note the exception for famous properties
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that if the property is well-known (e.g., 'the house of al-Walid in Kufa'), mentioning the name suffices without boundaries.
This exception also requires that witnesses know the property by name.
4
Evaluate the mufta bihi position
Despite the Sahibayn's view, the relied-upon opinion (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa: always mention four boundaries, whether the property is famous or not.
Tarjih authority: Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر حدوده: [الأربعة؛ لأن العقار يعرف به مع تعذر الإشارة إليه. (عيني)] الحدود جمع الحد، وهو ما يتميز العقار به عن غيره كالدور ،والأراضي، فالسوق والطريق والنهر لا يصلح . حداً؛ لأنه يزيد وينقص ويخرب، وذكر الحد المصر والمحلة والموضع وقيل: ذكر المحلة والسوق والسكة ليس بلازم، وذكر المصر والقرية لازم، وذكر حدوده الأربعة مطلقاً، سواء كان مشهوراً أو لا، وإليه مال أبو حنيفة وهو ظاهر الرواية. وقيل: إن كان مشهوراً كدار الوليد بالكوفة، ودار الفضل ببخارى، يكتفى بذكرها دون الحدود، وهو قولهما، إلا إذا عرفوا الشهود الدار بعينها فلا يحتاج إلى ذكر حدودها كما لو ادعى ثمن العقار؛ لأنه دعوى الدين حقيقة لا العقار. مع وكفت ثلاثة: أي لو ذكر المدعي ثلاثة من الحدود الأربعة كفاه، وكما يشترط ذكر الحد في الدعوى يشترط في الشهادة أيضاً؛ لأنه بما يصير معلوماً للقاضي، وقال زفر : لا يكفي ذكر الثلاثة بل لابد من ذكر الحدود الأربعة؛ لأن التعريف لا يتم إلا بها، ولهذا لو غلط في الرابعة لا تقبل، وبه قالت الثلاثة، ولنا أن للأكثر حكم الكل، بخلاف ما إذا غلط في الرابع؛ لأنه به يختلف المدعى، ولا يكون تركها كذلك، ونظيره إذا ادعى شراء شيء بثمن منقود، فإن الشهادة تقبل إن سكتوا عن بيان جنس الثمن، ولو ذكروا ذلك واختلفوا فيه لم تقبل. (عيني، فتح، زيلعي)
Mentioning its boundaries: [The four boundaries; because real estate is known by them when pointing to it is impossible. (Ayni)] Boundaries (hudud) is the plural of hadd, which is that by which real estate is distinguished from other properties, such as houses and lands. The market, road, and river are not valid as boundaries because they increase, decrease, and deteriorate. Mentioning the city, locality, and place [is required]; and it is said that mentioning the locality, market, and alley is not necessary, but mentioning the city and village is necessary. Mentioning all four boundaries in an absolute sense, whether the property is famous or not, is the view of Abu Hanifa and it is the apparent ruling (zahir al-riwayah). It is said: if it is famous, like the house of al-Walid in Kufa or the house of al-Fadl in Bukhara, mentioning its name suffices without the boundaries, which is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), unless the witnesses know the house itself, then there is no need to mention its boundaries, just as if one claims the price of real estate, because it is truly a claim of debt, not real estate. And three suffice: Meaning if the plaintiff mentions three of the four boundaries, it suffices. Just as mentioning the boundary is required in the claim, it is also required in testimony, because thereby it becomes known to the judge. Zufar said: mentioning three is not sufficient; rather all four boundaries must be mentioned, because identification is not complete except with them, and for this reason if one errs in the fourth it is not accepted, and this is the view of the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad). Our argument is that the majority has the ruling of the whole, unlike the case where he errs in the fourth, because thereby the claimed property changes, and omitting it is not like that. An analogy is if one claims to have bought something for a specified price, the testimony is accepted if they are silent about the type of price, but if they mention it and differ, it is not accepted. (Ayni, Fath, Zaylai)
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi perspective regarding the requirement of mentioning boundaries (hudud) in a claim for real estate. The text begins by defining boundaries as the features that distinguish a property, such as houses and lands, and notes that natural or public features like markets, roads, and rivers are invalid as boundaries due to their changeability. It states that mentioning the district, locality, and place is necessary, with some disagreement about the necessity of mentioning the market and alley. The relied-upon position (zahir al-riwayah) according to Abu Hanifa is that all four boundaries must be mentioned in all cases, whether the property is famous or not. However, the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that if the property is widely known, mentioning its name suffices, unless the witnesses are already familiar with the property. The passage then addresses the dispute over whether three boundaries suffice. Zufar requires all four, arguing that identification is incomplete without them. The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) hold that three suffice, applying the principle that the majority has the ruling of the whole. An example is given: in a claim for a purchased item, testimony is accepted even if the witness is silent on the type of price, but if they state it and contradict, it is rejected.

Key Terms
الحدود (al-hudud): The four boundaries defining a property in a claim (e.g., north, south, east, west).
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparent ruling transmitted from the founding imams in the six core books of Imam Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi Hanafi position, what must a plaintiff include in a claim for real estate?
A) Only the property's name if it is famous
B) All four boundaries even if the property is famous
C) Only three boundaries are sufficient
D) No boundaries are needed if the judge knows the property

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires boundaries for real estate claims; the mufta bihi holds that all four must be mentioned, even for famous properties.

Sufficiency of Three Boundaries: The Majority Principle
Discusses the dispute over three versus four boundaries

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi majority's position that three boundaries suffice in a real estate claim and evaluate Zufar's objection using the 'majority holds the ruling of the whole' principle.

Core Idea
Three boundaries suffice because the majority holds the ruling of the whole; error in the fourth changes the claim, but omission does not.

A dispute arises: if a plaintiff mentions only three of the four boundaries, is the claim valid? Zufar insists on all four, but the Hanafi majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) holds that three are enough, applying a fiqh principle. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and an analogy with price claims.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling of the majority
The text states that if the plaintiff mentions three boundaries, it is sufficient. This is the view of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
Zufar dissents, requiring all four.
2
Understand Zufar's objection
Zufar argues that identification is incomplete without all four; if the fourth is wrong, the claim changes. Therefore, omission should also be fatal.
Elsewhere it is reported that 'the three' (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) once held Zufar's view, but the later position is the majority.
3
Learn the Hanafi response
The Hanafi argument: 'al-akthar yufidu hukm al-kull' – the majority has the ruling of the whole. Omission of one boundary is not like error: error changes the subject, omission leaves it identifiable.
The analogy: in a claim of purchase, silence on the type of price is acceptable, but a contradictory statement invalidates the testimony.
4
Apply the principle to the analogy
Just as testimony is accepted if witnesses are silent on the price type (because the claim is still clear), mentioning three boundaries is acceptable because the property is sufficiently identified.
This reinforces that omission does not invalidate, while contradiction does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر حدوده: [الأربعة؛ لأن العقار يعرف به مع تعذر الإشارة إليه. (عيني)] الحدود جمع الحد، وهو ما يتميز العقار به عن غيره كالدور ،والأراضي، فالسوق والطريق والنهر لا يصلح . حداً؛ لأنه يزيد وينقص ويخرب، وذكر الحد المصر والمحلة والموضع وقيل: ذكر المحلة والسوق والسكة ليس بلازم، وذكر المصر والقرية لازم، وذكر حدوده الأربعة مطلقاً، سواء كان مشهوراً أو لا، وإليه مال أبو حنيفة وهو ظاهر الرواية. وقيل: إن كان مشهوراً كدار الوليد بالكوفة، ودار الفضل ببخارى، يكتفى بذكرها دون الحدود، وهو قولهما، إلا إذا عرفوا الشهود الدار بعينها فلا يحتاج إلى ذكر حدودها كما لو ادعى ثمن العقار؛ لأنه دعوى الدين حقيقة لا العقار. مع وكفت ثلاثة: أي لو ذكر المدعي ثلاثة من الحدود الأربعة كفاه، وكما يشترط ذكر الحد في الدعوى يشترط في الشهادة أيضاً؛ لأنه بما يصير معلوماً للقاضي، وقال زفر : لا يكفي ذكر الثلاثة بل لابد من ذكر الحدود الأربعة؛ لأن التعريف لا يتم إلا بها، ولهذا لو غلط في الرابعة لا تقبل، وبه قالت الثلاثة، ولنا أن للأكثر حكم الكل، بخلاف ما إذا غلط في الرابع؛ لأنه به يختلف المدعى، ولا يكون تركها كذلك، ونظيره إذا ادعى شراء شيء بثمن منقود، فإن الشهادة تقبل إن سكتوا عن بيان جنس الثمن، ولو ذكروا ذلك واختلفوا فيه لم تقبل. (عيني، فتح، زيلعي)
Mentioning its boundaries: [The four boundaries; because real estate is known by them when pointing to it is impossible. (Ayni)] Boundaries (hudud) is the plural of hadd, which is that by which real estate is distinguished from other properties, such as houses and lands. The market, road, and river are not valid as boundaries because they increase, decrease, and deteriorate. Mentioning the city, locality, and place [is required]; and it is said that mentioning the locality, market, and alley is not necessary, but mentioning the city and village is necessary. Mentioning all four boundaries in an absolute sense, whether the property is famous or not, is the view of Abu Hanifa and it is the apparent ruling (zahir al-riwayah). It is said: if it is famous, like the house of al-Walid in Kufa or the house of al-Fadl in Bukhara, mentioning its name suffices without the boundaries, which is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), unless the witnesses know the house itself, then there is no need to mention its boundaries, just as if one claims the price of real estate, because it is truly a claim of debt, not real estate. And three suffice: Meaning if the plaintiff mentions three of the four boundaries, it suffices. Just as mentioning the boundary is required in the claim, it is also required in testimony, because thereby it becomes known to the judge. Zufar said: mentioning three is not sufficient; rather all four boundaries must be mentioned, because identification is not complete except with them, and for this reason if one errs in the fourth it is not accepted, and this is the view of the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad). Our argument is that the majority has the ruling of the whole, unlike the case where he errs in the fourth, because thereby the claimed property changes, and omitting it is not like that. An analogy is if one claims to have bought something for a specified price, the testimony is accepted if they are silent about the type of price, but if they mention it and differ, it is not accepted. (Ayni, Fath, Zaylai)

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi perspective regarding the requirement of mentioning boundaries (hudud) in a claim for real estate. The text begins by defining boundaries as the features that distinguish a property, such as houses and lands, and notes that natural or public features like markets, roads, and rivers are invalid as boundaries due to their changeability. It states that mentioning the district, locality, and place is necessary, with some disagreement about the necessity of mentioning the market and alley. However, the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that if the property is widely known, mentioning its name suffices, unless the witnesses are already familiar with the property. An example is given: in a claim for a purchased item, testimony is accepted even if the witness is silent on the type of price, but if they state it and contradict, it is rejected.

Key Terms
الأكثر (al-akthar): The majority (of boundaries); used in the principle that the majority holds the ruling of the whole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi majority, what is the minimum number of boundaries a plaintiff must mention in a real estate claim?
A) One boundary
B) Two boundaries
C) Three boundaries
D) Four boundaries

Key Takeaway
Three boundaries are sufficient for a real estate claim because the majority principle applies; error in a boundary invalidates, but omission does not.

Naming Boundary Owners: The Grandfather Requirement in Property Claims
Intra-Hanafi dispute on necessity of lineage in boundary identification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi requirements for identifying boundary owners in a real estate claim and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa insists on the grandfather in boundary identification; disciples relax the rule for famous persons or properties.

In a Hanafi court, when a plaintiff describes a property by its boundaries, the owners of adjacent properties must be identified with sufficient clarity to avoid confusion. This lesson examines the ruling that the plaintiff must mention not only the names but also the lineages (including the grandfather) of those boundary owners, and the intra-Hanafi dispute on when this detail is required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core requirement
The text states that in a real estate claim, the plaintiff must mention the names of the owners of the boundaries and their lineages (ancestry) to achieve proper identification.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that mentioning the grandfather (father's father) is necessary unless the person is widely known among the people. If the owner is not famous, the grandfather must be included to remove ambiguity.
The condition 'not famous' triggers the grandfather requirement.
3
Analyze the disciples' position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two disciples) hold that if the property itself is as famous as the owner, the boundaries need not be specified; and if the owner is famous, mentioning his name alone suffices without the grandfather.
The disciples focus on the fame of the property or owner as a sufficient means of identification.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
The difference affects the rigor of identification in claims. Abu Hanifa's view is more cautious, requiring lineage unless fame is established. The disciples' view is more lenient, allowing fame to substitute for detailed lineage.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's position, as it is more cautious.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأسماء أصحابها: أي وذكر أسماء أصحاب الحدود؛ لأن التعريف يحصل بذلك، وذكر أيضاً أنسابهم ليتميزوا عن غيرهم. ولابد من ذكر الجد : أي أب الأب فيقول في كل أحد ملك فلان بن فلان بشرط أن لا يكون الرجل مشهوراً بين الناس؛ لأن تمام التعريف يحصل بذكر الجد، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة؛ وعندهما: إذا كان العقار مشهوراً شهرة الرجل لا يحتاج إلى تحديده، وإن كان الرجل مشهوراً يكتفى بذكره؛ الحصول المقصود وهو ارتفاع الاشتراك. رمز الحقائق: ۱۸۷/۲] (نقاية)
And the names of their owners: i.e., mentioning the names of the owners of the boundaries, because identification is achieved by that, and also mentioning their lineages to distinguish them from others. And it is necessary to mention the grandfather: i.e., the father's father, so that one says for each: 'the property of so-and-so son of so-and-so', on condition that the man is not famous among people, because complete identification is achieved by mentioning the grandfather. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): if the real estate is as famous as the man, he does not need to specify it, and if the man is famous, mentioning him suffices to achieve the purpose, which is to remove ambiguity. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 187/2) (Niqayah)
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement about the requirements in a claim for real estate involving the identification of boundaries and the plaintiff/defendant. The text explains that the plaintiff must mention not only the boundaries but also the names of the owners of those adjacent properties, including their lineages, to ensure accurate identification. Additionally, the grandfather (father's father) must be mentioned unless the person is widely known. This is the position of Abu Hanifa. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that if the property is as famous as the owner, no specification is needed, and if the owner is famous, mentioning him suffices without the grandfather. The purpose is to remove any ambiguity about the identity of the property.

Key Terms
الجد (al-jadd): The paternal grandfather of the boundary owner, whose mention is required for identification unless the owner is famous.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi ruling in the Hanafi school, when is it necessary to mention the grandfather of a boundary owner in a real estate claim?
A) Always, regardless of fame.
B) Only if the owner is not famous among the people.
C) Only if the property is not famous.
D) Never, because the two disciples' view is preferred.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi real estate claims, identifying boundary owners requires their names and lineages; Abu Hanifa's position (grandfather unless famous) is the relied-upon ruling.

Proving Possession of Real Estate in Claims
States possession requirement and proof method for real estate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for proving possession of real estate in a claim, distinguish it from movable property, and identify the exceptions for claims of usurpation or purchase.

Core Idea
Possession of real estate is not proven by mutual agreement; evidence or judge's knowledge is required to prevent collusion.

In a claim for real estate, it is crucial to establish that the defendant is the possessor. This lesson explains how possession is proven according to the Hanafi school. You will learn why mutual agreement is insufficient and what proofs are acceptable, along with exceptions for certain types of claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the requirement
What must the plaintiff state in a claim for real estate? Why is possession relevant? The plaintiff must mention the boundaries and that the property is in the defendant's hand; the defendant is only a proper party if he possesses it.
This is wajib (obligatory) in the claim.
2
Understand the proof of possession
How is possession of real estate proven? It is not proven by mutual agreement between plaintiff and defendant; instead, it must be proven by the plaintiff's evidence (bayyinah) or the judge's personal knowledge.
This rule prevents collusion.
3
Contrast with movable property
For movable property, mutual agreement suffices because possession is visible.
Movable property is 'mushahadah' (observed).
4
Distinguish types of claims
For claims of absolute ownership, possession must be proven. But for claims of usurpation (ghasb) or purchase from the possessor, evidence is not limited to the plaintiff; claims of action are valid against anyone.
The passage notes this exception briefly.
5
Apply the procedure
If the claim is valid, the judge asks the defendant; if he admits, judgment is given; if he denies and plaintiff provides evidence, judgment is given; otherwise the defendant takes an oath.
This is the standard procedure in claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنه في يده عطف على قوله: "ذكر حدوده" أي وذكر أيضاً أن العقار في يد المدعى عليه؛ لأن المدعى عليه لا يكون خصماً إلا إذا كان العقار في يده، فلابد من ذكره، ولا يحتاج إلى هذا القيد في المنقول؛ لأنه مشاهد في اليد. [رمز الحقائق: ۱۸۷/۲] بتصادقهما : أي بتصادق المدعي والمدعى عليه بأن ذكر المدعي: أن العقار المدعى في يد المدعى عليه فصدقه المدعى عليه في ذلك فلا يثبت اليد بهذا؛ لأن اليد فيه غير مشاهدة، ولعله في يد هما، وهما تواضعا فيه؛ ليكون لهما ذريعة إلى أخذه بحكم الحاكم. غيره  كتاب الدعوى 7 أحكام الدعوى بل ببينة أو علم القاضي، بخلاف المنقول، وأنه يطالبه به، وإن كان دينا ذكر العقار في الذمة المدعي وصفه وأنه يطالبه به فإن صحت الدعوى سأل المدعى عليه عنها، فإن أقر أو على وجه المذكور أي القاضي أنكر، فبرهن المدعي قضى عليه، وإلا حلف أي المدعي أي أقام البينة على دعواه أي القاضي بلا طلب المدعي أي القاضي المدعى عليه القاضي أي عن الدعوى بل ببينة: أي بل يثبت اليد ببينة المدعي؛ لأن المدعى عليه لا يكون خصماً إلا إذا كان العقار في يده، فلابد من إثباته، وهذا إذا ادعى ملكاً مطلقاً، أما في دعوى الغصب ودعوى الشراء من ذي اليد فلا يقتصر إلى البينة؛ لأن دعوى الفعل كما تصح على ذي اليد تصح على غيره أيضاً أو علم القاضي: [باليد في الصحيح؛ لاحتمال تزويرهما. (ط)] فإنه بمنزلة حجة إلا في بعض الأحكام، والمعنى: أو يثبت اليد بعلم القاضي؛ لتنتفي تهمة المواضعة. رمز الحقائق: ۱۸۷/۲] بخلاف المنقول: فإنه يثبت فيه اليد بتصادقهما ؛ لأن اليد فيه معاينة، فلا حاجة إلى اشتراط الزيادة. (ط، عيني)
And that it is in his possession: is connected to his saying 'mentioning its boundaries', i.e., also mentioning that the real estate is in the possession of the defendant, because the defendant is only a party if the property is in his possession, so it must be mentioned. This condition is not needed for movable property because it is observed in the hand. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 187/2] By their mutual agreement: i.e., by the mutual agreement of the plaintiff and defendant that the plaintiff states that the claimed property is in the defendant's hand and the defendant confirms it, possession is not proven by this, because possession of real estate is not observed, and perhaps it is in both their hands, and they have colluded in it to use it as a means to take it by the judge's ruling. Other than that. [Page 7] Book of Claims 7 Rulings of Claims But by evidence or the judge's knowledge, unlike movable property, and that he demands it from him, and if it is a debt, he mentions the property in the dhimmah, the plaintiff describes it and that he demands it. Then if the claim is valid, he asks the defendant about it; if he admits or denies, then the plaintiff provides evidence, the judge rules against him, otherwise he administers an oath. That is, the plaintiff, i.e., he presents evidence on his claim, i.e., the judge, without the plaintiff's request, i.e., the judge, the defendant, the judge, i.e., about the claim. But by evidence: i.e., rather, possession is proven by the plaintiff's evidence, because the defendant is only a party if the property is in his possession, so it must be proven. This is when claiming absolute ownership. As for a claim of usurpation (ghasb) or a claim of purchase from the possessor, it is not limited to evidence, because a claim of an action is valid against the possessor as well as others. Or by the judge's knowledge: [of the possession in the correct view; due to the possibility of their collusion. (T)] It is like a proof except in some rulings. The meaning: or possession is proven by the judge's knowledge to negate the suspicion of collusion. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 187/2] Unlike movable property: for it, possession is proven by their mutual agreement, because possession of it is visible, so there is no need to require additional proof. (T, Ayni)
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the requirement of proving possession in a claim for real estate. The text explains that in a claim, the plaintiff must state that the property is in the defendant's possession, because only the possessor is the proper defendant. For movable property, this condition is unnecessary since possession is obvious. A key point: possession of real estate cannot be established simply by the mutual agreement of the plaintiff and defendant, because that could be collusion to obtain the property through a court order. Instead, possession must be proven by evidence (bayyinah) from the plaintiff or by the judge's own knowledge. The judge's knowledge is accepted in the correct view to prevent collusion. For movable property, mutual agreement suffices because possession is visible. The passage also distinguishes between claims of absolute ownership (where possession must be proven) and claims of usurpation or purchase from the possessor (where evidence is not limited to the plaintiff). This is followed by a brief overview of the subsequent procedure: if the claim is valid, the judge asks the defendant; if the defendant admits, the judge rules accordingly; if he denies and the plaintiff provides evidence, the judge rules; otherwise the defendant takes an oath.

Key Terms
اليد (al-yad): Possession; the fact that the property is under the defendant's control.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How is possession of real estate proven in a claim according to the Hanafi school?
A) By the mutual agreement of plaintiff and defendant
B) By the plaintiff's evidence or the judge's knowledge
C) By the defendant's admission alone
D) It does not need to be proven

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, possession of real estate must be proven by evidence or judge's knowledge, not by mutual agreement, to prevent collusion.

Requirements for a Claim of Debt: Description and Demand in Hanafi Law
Requires describing debt and mentioning demand in claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions (shurut) for a claim of debt in Hanafi fiqh and identify the wajib elements required from the plaintiff.

Core Idea
A debt claim without proper description and demand is invalid — the plaintiff must specify the debt's cause and attributes.

When someone claims a debt from another, the Hanafi school imposes strict procedural requirements to ensure clarity and prevent false claims. This lesson unpacks the ruling from Kanz al-Daqaiq that a plaintiff must both describe the debt in detail and explicitly state that he demands it. Without these, the judge cannot proceed with the case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Subject of the Ruling
Recognize that the passage deals with the claim (da'wa) for a debt (dayn), which is a liability in the dhimmah.
Debt is not physically present, so precise description is necessary.
2
State the Two Wajib Requirements
The plaintiff must (1) describe the debt (wasf) and (2) mention the demand (talab). Both are wajib for the claim to be heard.
Omitting either renders the claim procedurally deficient.
3
List the Elements of Description
The description must include genus (jins), type (naw'), quality (sifah), quantity (qadr), and cause (sabab al-wujub).
Example: 'I sold him a car for 10,000 dirhams, payment due last month, and he has not paid.'
4
Understand the Procedural Flow
After a valid claim, the judge asks the defendant. If he admits, judgment follows; if he denies and the plaintiff provides evidence, the judge rules; otherwise the defendant takes an oath.
The description/demand occur at the claim stage, before the defendant's response.
5
Note the Consequence of Omission
If the plaintiff fails to describe the debt or state the demand, the claim is not validly heard — the judge cannot proceed.
The claim may be dismissed or returned for correction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنه يطالبه به، وإن كان دينا ذكر العقار في الذمة المدعي وصفه وأنه يطالبه به فإن صحت الدعوى سأل المدعى عليه عنها، فإن أقر أو على وجه المذكور أي القاضي أنكر، فبرهن المدعي قضى عليه، وإلا حلف أي المدعي أي أقام البينة على دعواه أي القاضي بلا طلب المدعي أي القاضي المدعى عليه القاضي أي عن الدعوى
And that he demands it from him, and if it is a debt, he mentions the property in the dhimmah, the plaintiff describes it and that he demands it from him. Then if the claim is valid, he asks the defendant about it; if he admits or on the mentioned face, i.e., the judge denies, then the plaintiff provides evidence, the judge rules against him, otherwise he administers an oath. That is, the plaintiff, i.e., he presents evidence on his claim, i.e., the judge without the plaintiff's request, i.e., the judge, the defendant, the judge, i.e., about the claim.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement about the requirements for a claim of debt. The text begins by stating that the plaintiff must mention that he demands the owed property from the defendant. If the claim is for a debt (dayn), the plaintiff must describe the debt by specifying its type, quality, quantity, and cause of obligation, and also state that he demands it. The passage then briefly summarizes the procedure for all claims: after the claim is made, the judge asks the defendant; if the defendant admits, the judge rules accordingly; if he denies and the plaintiff provides evidence, the judge rules; otherwise the defendant takes an oath. The text includes some broken commentary indicating that the judge administers the oath without the plaintiff's request. The commentary following (which is not fully preserved here) expands on the requirement to describe the debt in terms of its genus, type, quality, quantity, and cause.

Key Terms
الدين (al-dayn): A debt; a liability that is not physically present but owed in the dhimmah.
المطالبة (al-mutalaba): The demand; the plaintiff's explicit request for the owed property.
الوصف (al-wasf): Description; specifying the attributes of the debt: genus, type, quality, quantity, cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only the amount
B) Only the cause of the debt
C) A full description including genus, type, quality, quantity, and cause, plus a statement of demand
D) Only the defendant's name

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a claim for debt is only valid if the plaintiff describes the debt in full (genus, type, quality, quantity, cause) and explicitly demands it.

Emphasis of Oath by Attributes, Not by Time or Place
Inter-madhhab dispute on oath emphasis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on oath emphasis, state the opposing Shafi'i view, and identify the usul argument (naskh) used by the Hanafis.

Core Idea
Oath emphasis is by Allah's attributes only; adding time or place is an unauthorized addition to the text.

When a defendant denies a claim, the judge may solemnly emphasize the oath to deter falsehood. The Hanafi school holds that this emphasis must be by mentioning Allah's attributes, not by invoking specific times or places. This lesson unpacks the rule, explores the reasons, and contrasts it with the Shafi'i position that permits time/place emphasis for serious cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? The emphasis of the oath (taghliz). What is its hukm? According to Hanafis, it is permissible (mubah) but not obligatory. Note: the oath itself is obligatory on the defendant, but emphasis is extra.
The passage first states the rule: emphasis by attributes, not by time/place.
2
State the Hanafi position and its proof
The Hanafi position: the oath is emphasized by mentioning Allah's attributes (without the waw to avoid repetition). The proof is the hadith of Ali: 'The oath is upon the one who denies,' which is general and unqualified. Adding time/place would be an addition to the text, amounting to abrogation (naskh) without proof.
The Hanafis argue that any specification of time/place would exceed the general command and thus is invalid.
3
State the opposing school's position and proof
Al-Shafi'i (and Malik, Ahmad in one narration) allows emphasis by place (e.g., between the Kaaba's corner and station in Makkah, by the Prophet's grave in Madinah) and by time (Friday after Asr) for major cases like qasamah, li'an, or large wealth. Their proof is istihsan and practice, aiming at greater deterrence.
The Shafi'i view is based on practical benefit, not strict textual interpretation.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that such specification would distort the apparent meaning of the hadith ('al-yaminu 'ala man ankara') and is akin to unauthorized abrogation (naskh). They do not accept istihsan overriding a clear general text.
This reveals the usul principle: a general text cannot be restricted without evidence.
5
Determine the practical difference
In Hanafi courts, oaths are never administered at specific sacred sites or times; in Shafi'i courts, they may be for large claims. The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is the negation of time/place emphasis.
The thamarah al-khilaf is a real procedural difference in court practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كيفية اليمين والاستحلاف
كتاب الدعوى وتغلّظ بذكر أوصافه لا بزمان ومكان. بذكر أوصافه [أي بذكر أوصاف الله بدون حرف العطف؛ لئلا تكرر اليمين] مثل أن يقول: بالله الذي لا إله إلا هو عالم الغيب والشهادة، هو الرحمن الرحيم الذي يعلم من السر ويعلم من العلانية ما لفلان هذا علي ولا قبلي هذا المال الذي ادعاه، وهو كذا وكذا، وإنما تغلظ بذكر الأوصاف؛ لأن منهم من يمتنع عن اليمين بالتغليظ ويتجاسر عند عدمه فيغلظ عليه لعله يمتنع بذلك، ولو لم يغلظ جاز، وقيل: لا يغلظ على الإنسان المعروف بالصلاح ويغلظ على غيره، وقيل: يغلظ في الخطير من المال دون الحقير. فلو غلظ فحلف من غير تغليظ ونكل، التغليظ لا يقضى عليه بالنكول؛ لأن المقصود الحلف بالله وقد حصل، وفيما إذا غلظ بذكر الصفة يحترز عن الإتيان بالواو لئلا يتكرر اليمين.
لا بزمان ومكان: أي لا تغلظ اليمين على المسلم بزمان ومكان مطلقاً؛ لأن في التغليظ بالزمان تأخير حق المدعي في اليمين إلى ذلك الزمان وكذا بالمكان؛ لأن فيه التأخير إلى الوصول إلى ذلك المكان المغلظ به فلا يشرع. وظاهر "الهداية": أن المنفي وجوب التغليظ فيكون مشروعاً، وفي "المحيط": ما يدل على إباحته ولكن ذكر أنه لا يجوز التغليظ بالمكان، وقال الشافعي: إن كان اليمين في قسامة أو لعان أو في مال عظيم يبلغ مائتي مثقال يغلظ بالمكان، فيحلف بين الركن والمقام إن كان بمكة، وعند قبر النبي عليه إن كان في المدينة، وعند الصخرة إن كان في بيت المقدس، وفي الجامع إن كان في غيرها، وإن لم يكن الجامع ففي المساجد ويكون ذلك الجمعة بعد العصر، وبه قال مالك وأحمد في رواية. ولنا: إطلاق قوله علي: "اليمين على من أنكر"، والتخصيص بالزمان أو المكان زيادة على النص، وهو نسخ.
The manner of the oath and requiring the oath. The book of claims. And it is emphasized by mentioning His attributes, not by time or place. By mentioning His attributes: i.e., by mentioning the attributes of Allah without the conjunction waw; so that the oath is not repeated. For example, he says: By Allah, there is no god but He, Knower of the unseen and the witnessed, He is the Most Merciful, the Most Compassionate, who knows the secret and knows the public, that this person has no claim upon me nor do I owe this property that he claims, and it is such and such. And it is only emphasized by mentioning the attributes because some of them refrain from the oath with emphasis and become bold when it is absent, so emphasis is placed upon him so that he might refrain by that. And if he did not emphasize, it is permissible. And it is said: do not emphasize upon a person known for righteousness, and emphasize upon others. And it is said: emphasize in significant wealth, not insignificant. If he emphasized and he swore without emphasis and then refused, the emphasis is not ruled against him for refusal because the purpose is the oath by Allah, and it has occurred. And when he emphasizes by mentioning the attribute, he refrains from bringing the waw so that the oath is not repeated. 'Not by time or place': i.e., the oath is not emphasized upon the Muslim by time or place absolutely, because emphasis by time delays the plaintiff's right to the oath to that time, and likewise by place because it delays until reaching that place of emphasis, so it is not legislated. The apparent of al-Hidayah is that the obligation of emphasis is negated, so it is prescribed. In al-Muhit, that which indicates its permissibility, but it is mentioned that emphasis by place is not permissible. Al-Shafi'i said: If the oath is in a qasamah or li'an or in great wealth reaching two hundred mithqals, it is emphasized by place: he swears between the corner and the station if in Makkah, by the grave of the Prophet (peace be upon him) if in Madinah, by the rock if in Jerusalem, in the congregational mosque if elsewhere, and if there is no congregational mosque then in the mosques, and that on Friday after Asr. And Malik and Ahmad said likewise in a narration. Our evidence: the general statement of Ali: 'The oath is upon the one who denies.' And specification by time or place is an addition to the text, and it is abrogation.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq delineates the Hanafi rules for emphasizing an oath in courtroom proceedings. The passage type is a ruling with inter-madhhab dispute. The text first states the core rule: emphasis is done by mentioning Allah's attributes, not by time or place. The commentator explains that when the judge emphasizes the oath, he should describe Allah's attributes without using the conjunction 'waw' (and) to avoid repeating the oath. Examples include phrases like 'by Allah, there is no god but He, Knower of the unseen and witnessed...'. The reason for this emphasis is psychological: some people are deterred by a solemn oath in Allah's names, while they might be brazen with a simple one. Emphasis is not required; if the judge does not emphasize, the oath is still valid. There are two opinions: one says not to emphasize on a pious person, another says emphasize only for significant amounts of property. If the judge emphasized but the claimant swore without the emphasized wording and then refused, the refusal is not cause for judgment because the oath by Allah was already made. The phrase 'لا بزمان ومكان' is then commented upon: the oath upon a Muslim is not to be emphasized by specific times or places absolutely, as that would delay the plaintiff's right. The Hedaya explains that the negation is of obligation, so it is still permissible if not mandatory. Al-Muhit indicates permissibility but states that emphasis by place is not allowed. The Shafi'i school, along with Malik and Ahmad in one narration, allows emphasis by place under certain conditions: qasamah (repeated oaths), li'an (imprecation), or for very large debts, requiring the oath at the Kaaba's corner, the Prophet's grave, the Rock in Jerusalem, or the mosque on Friday after Asr. The Hanafi rebuttal is the hadith of Ali: 'The oath is upon the one who denies' — adding time/place is an unauthorized addition amounting to abrogation. The Hanafi position is clear: emphasis by time/place is not prescribed, though the plain text allows it as permissible but not recommended.

Key Terms
التغليظ (al-taghlīẓ): Emphasizing the oath by invoking Allah's attributes or solemn circumstances to deter false swearing.
النكول (al-nukūl): Refusal of the defendant to take the oath after it is required of him, leading to judgment against him.
اليمين (al-yamīn): The oath sworn by the defendant to deny the plaintiff's claim.
النسخ (al-naskh): Abrogation of a ruling by a later text; here, the Hanafi argument that specifying time/place would abrogate the general hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is NOT a valid method of oath emphasis?
A) Swearing by the attributes of Allah without using the conjunction 'and'
B) Swearing in a mosque on Friday after Asr
C) Swearing in a court before the judge
D) Swearing by the name of Allah alone

Key Takeaway
Emphasis by time or place is not part of Hanafi practice; only by Allah's attributes is the prescribed method.

Oaths for Jews, Christians, Magians, and Idolaters
Rules for administering oaths to non-Muslim litigants according to their beliefs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for administering oaths to non-Muslims (Jew, Christian, Magian, idolater) and identify the rationale behind each formula.

Core Idea
Each non-Muslim's oath is tailored to their veneration, and never in their place of worship.

In Hanafi courts, when a non-Muslim litigant must swear an oath, the judge customizes the formula to match what the witness holds sacred. This lesson unpacks the rules for Jews, Christians, Magians, and idolaters, as detailed in Kanz al-Daqaiq and its commentary. You will learn the precise wording for each group and the reasoning behind it, as well as where the oath may be administered.

Guided Path
1
Identify the formula for each group
List the four categories of non-Muslims (Jew, Christian, Magian, idolater) and the exact oath formula prescribed for each in the text.
The formulas are: Jew – by Allah who revealed the Torah to Moses; Christian – by Allah who revealed the Gospel to Jesus; Magian – by Allah who created the fire; idolater – by Allah alone.
2
Understand the rationale for each formula
For each group, note what they venerate (e.g., Torah, Gospel, fire) and how the formula references that veneration to make the oath solemn.
The Jew venerates the Torah, so the Torah is mentioned. The Christian venerates the Gospel. The Magian venerates fire, so the Creator of fire is mentioned. The idolater worships other than Allah, so only Allah is mentioned to avoid association.
3
Recognize the prohibition on places of worship
The text states oaths are not administered in their places of worship (e.g., churches, synagogues) because it glorifies them.
The judge is prohibited from entering such places, and even a Muslim entering them is makruh tahrimi and may be subject to ta'zir.
4
Note the ruling on Muslim attendance
The commentary adds that entering a church or synagogue is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi) and may warrant discretionary punishment (ta'zir).
Al-Bahr mentions a fatwa for ta'zir of a Muslim who frequents the church with Jews.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحلف اليهودي: بالله الذي أنزل التوراة على موسى والنصراني بالله الذي أنزل الإنجيل على عيسى. لأن اليهودي يعظم التوراة، ويستحلف النصراني لأنه يعظم الإنجيل. والمجوسي بالله الذي خلق النار والوثني بالله ولا يحلفون في بيوت عبادتهم، ويحلف أي هؤلاء المذكورون. والمجوسي: أي ويستحلف المجوسي، وهو الذي يعبد النار. خلق النار لأنهم يعظمون النار، وتؤكد اليمين عليهم بذكر خالقها. والوثني: أي ويستحلف الوثني، وهو الذي يعبد غير الله ويعتقد أن الله. بالله تعالى فقط ولا يذكر معه شيئاً آخر. والحاصل أن اليمين تغلظ على كل بما يعتقد. في بيوت عبادتهم لأن فيها تعظيما، والقاضي ممنوع عن حضورها. وهي الكنائس والبيع؛ لأن القاضي لا يحضرها بل هو ممنوع من ذلك، بل لكل مسلم يكره الدخول في البيعة والكنيسة؛ لأنهما مجمع الشياطين، والظاهر: أن الكراهة تحريمية، قال في "البحر": وقد أفتيت بتعزير مسلم لازم الكنيسة مع اليهود.
And the Jew is required to swear: by Allah who revealed the Torah to Musa, and the Christian by Allah who revealed the Gospel to Isa. Because the Jew venerates the Torah, and the Christian is required to swear because he venerates the Gospel. And the Magian by Allah who created the fire, and the idolater by Allah, and they are not sworn in their places of worship, and they swear, any of these mentioned. And the Magian: i.e., the Magian is required to swear, and he is the fire worshipper. 'Who created the fire' because they venerate fire, and the oath is emphasized upon them by mentioning its Creator. And the idolater: i.e., the idolater is required to swear, and he is one who worships other than Allah and believes in Allah(?). By Allah the Exalted only, and nothing else is mentioned with Him. The summary is that the oath is emphasized upon each according to what he believes. Not in their places of worship because in that is glorification, and the judge is prohibited from attending them. They are churches and synagogues; because the judge does not attend them, rather he is prohibited from that. Rather, every Muslim dislikes entering the synagogue or church because they are gathering places of devils, and the apparent is that the dislike is prohibitively disliked. He said in al-Bahr: I have issued a fatwa that a Muslim who frequents the church with the Jews is to be punished.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq and its commentary addresses how oaths are administered to non-Muslim litigants in a Hanafi court. The passage type is a ruling with proof based on customary veneration. The matn states: the Jew is sworn by 'Allah who revealed the Torah to Musa', the Christian by 'Allah who revealed the Gospel to Isa', the Magian (fire-worshipper) by 'Allah who created the fire', and the idolater by 'Allah' alone. The commentator explains the reasoning: each group venerates their scripture or object, so the oath is framed accordingly to make it solemn for them. For the Magian, mentioning the Creator of fire reminds them that their worshipped element is created. For the idolater, only Allah is mentioned to avoid any association. The rule is that the oath is emphasized according to what the person believes. Additionally, these oaths are not to be administered in their places of worship (churches, synagogues) because that would glorify them, and the judge is prohibited from entering such places. Even for a Muslim, entering a church or synagogue is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi), as they are considered gatherings of devils. The author of al-Bahr states he issued a fatwa for ta'zir (discretionary punishment) for a Muslim who frequents the church with Jews.

Key Terms
المجوسي (al-majūsī): A Zoroastrian fire-worshipper, sworn by Allah who created the fire.
الوثني (al-wathanī): An idolater who worships something other than Allah; sworn by Allah alone without any addition.
البيعة (al-bīʿa): Church or synagogue; places where non-Muslims worship, which Muslims are prohibited from entering for the purpose of swearing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) By the fire itself
B) By Allah who created the fire
C) By Allah alone
D) By the Zoroastrian scripture

Key Takeaway
Non-Muslims are sworn by Allah in a manner tailored to their beliefs, and oaths are not administered in their places of worship.

6 min
Swearing on the Result (al-Hāsil) in Claims
Defendants swear on the current legal effect, not the original cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of swearing on the resulting legal effect (al-hāsil) in claims of sale, marriage, usurpation, and divorce, and distinguish it from swearing on the original cause (al-sabab).

Core Idea
The defendant swears on the current legal outcome, not the original act, to avoid perjury when the cause may have been nullified.

When a defendant denies a claim, the oath must reflect the truth. But what if the original event (like a sale) occurred but was later cancelled? The Hanafi school teaches that the oath should be on the present legal effect, not the past cause. This lesson unpacks the reasoning from Kanz al-Daqaiq, showing how this principle protects defendants from forced perjury while still serving the plaintiff's true objective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is swearing in a legal claim. The ruling is that it is wajib (necessary) for the judge to administer the oath on the result (al-hāsil) when the cause can be nullified.
This is the Hanafi position as stated by al-Zayla'i.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the principle: 'The purpose of causes is their legal effects' (المقصود من الأسباب أحكامها). This implies that swearing on the effect fulfills the plaintiff's true goal.
The plaintiff's real interest is the current ruling, not the historical event.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The link between the principle and the ruling: Since the plaintiff's purpose is the current legal consequence, denying the consequence is sufficient. The defendant need not deny the cause, which may have been nullified.
For example, in a sale claim, the plaintiff wants the item or money now; a prior sale that was revoked has no current effect.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some scholars might allow swearing on the cause directly. The Hanafis restrict to the result for these four types of claims because the cause can be lifted.
The text says 'al-azhar' (the most apparent) indicating this is the preferred view.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is swearing on the result, as it balances the rights of both parties without harming either.
Al-Zayla'i in Tabyin al-Haqa'iq supports this as the apparent view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويحلف على صورة إنكار المنكر، وهو صورة دعوى المدعي. وفي "الخلاصة": أي حاصل الدعوى، وقال الزيلعي: المقصود من الأسباب أحكامها، فيحلف على نفي الأحكام لا على نفي الأسباب. وظاهره أن الحاصل هو الحكم المرتب على السبب، وهو الأظهر. ثم اعلم أن إدخال النكاح في المسائل التي يحلف فيها على الحاصل عند الطرفين محمول على ما إذا كان مع النكاح دعوى المال. وقوله: "ما هي بائن منك"؛ لأنه خاص بالبائن، وأما الرجعي فيحلف بالله ما هي خالق في النكاح الذي بينكما. وقوله: "في دعوى البيع والنكاح والغصب والطلاق" أي يحلف على الحاصل في هذه الصور مطلقاً. مثال: ادعى أنه ابتاع هذا عبداً بألف فجحد، حلف بالله ما بينكما بيع قائم، ولا يحلف بالله ما بعت؛ فلعله باعه ثم أقاله. فلو حلف على نفي أصل السبب يكون كاذباً، ولو لم يحلف يجب تسليم المبيع العائد إلى ملكه بالإقالة؛ لأنه لو ادعى المعنى الطارئ لا يقبل منه. فإذا حلف على الحاصل فقد وفى للمدعي والمدعى عليه حقهما؛ لأن مقصود المدعي من دعوى البيع ونحوه ثبوت الحكم، ومتى أمكن إيفاء حقهما كان أولى من إيفاء حق أحدهما وإتلاف حق الآخر. والأصل: أن الدعوى إذا وقعت في سبب يرتفع بعد وقوعه، وليس في تحليفه على الحاصل ضرر بالمدعي كالصور المذكورة: فإن البيع يرتفع بالإقالة، والغصب يرتفع بالرد وبالهبة، والنكاح بالطلاق والخلع، والطلاق بتجديد العقد أو الرجعة. فلا يحلف بالله ما نكحت، ولا بالله ما بعت، ولا بالله ما غصبت، ولا بالله ما طلقت؛ لأن هذه الأشياء قد تقع ثم ترتفع برافع فلا يمكن تحليفه على السبب فيحلفه على الحاصل؛ لكي لا يتضرر المدعى عليه؛ لأنه لو أقر بالسبب ثم ادعى طروء الرافع لا يقبل منه. فيحتال بهذا الطريق؛ إذ لا ضرر فيه على المدعي؛ لأن المقصود من الأسباب أحكامها فيحلف على نفيها لا على نفي السبب، إلا إذا كان في التحليف على الحاصل ترك النظر.
And he swears upon the form of denial of the defendant, which is the form of the plaintiff's claim. In al-Khulasah: i.e., the substance of the claim. Al-Zayla'i said: The purpose of causes is their legal effects, so he swears upon the negation of the effects, not upon the negation of the causes. The apparent meaning is that the resulting effect is the ruling attached to the cause, and that is the most apparent. Then know that including marriage among the cases where the oath is upon the resulting effect according to both parties is carried upon the situation when with the marriage there is a claim for property. And his statement: 'She is not irrevocably divorced from you' because it is specific to irrevocable divorce; as for revocable divorce, he swears by Allah that she is not separate in the marriage between you two. And his statement: 'In claims of sale, marriage, usurpation, and divorce' means he swears upon the resulting effect in these cases absolutely. Example: He claims that he bought this slave for a thousand, and the defendant denies; he swears by Allah that there is no standing sale between you two, and he does not swear by Allah that he did not sell; because perhaps he sold it and then revoked the sale. If he swears upon the negation of the original cause, he would be lying, and if he does not swear, he must hand over the sold item that returned to his ownership through revocation because if he claims the subsequent event, it is not accepted from him. So when he swears upon the resulting effect, he fulfills the rights of both the plaintiff and the defendant; because we know that the plaintiff's purpose in claims of sale and the like is the establishment of the ruling, and whenever it is possible to fulfill the rights of both, it is better than fulfilling the right of one and destroying the right of the other. The principle is that when the claim falls upon a cause that can be lifted after it occurs, there is no harm to the plaintiff in swearing him upon the resulting effect, such as the mentioned examples: sale is lifted by revocation, usurpation by return or gift, marriage by divorce or khula', divorce by renewal of the marriage contract or return (raj'a). So he does not swear by Allah that he did not marry, nor that he did not sell, nor that he did not usurp, nor that he did not divorce; because these things may occur and then be lifted by a remover, so he cannot be sworn upon the cause. So he is sworn upon the resulting effect, so that the defendant is not harmed; because if he confessed the cause and then claimed the occurrence of the remover, it would not be accepted from him. So this method is employed as a stratagem, since there is no harm in it upon the plaintiff; because the purpose of causes is their rulings, so he swears upon their negation, not upon the negation of the cause, unless in swearing upon the resulting effect there is a disregard of consideration.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq explains a crucial Hanafi principle: when a defendant is required to swear in certain claims (sale, marriage, usurpation, divorce), he swears on the current legal outcome (al-hāsil), not on the original event (al-sabab). The passage type is a ruling with proof based on the purpose of legal causes. The commentator (likely al-Zayla'i) states that the purpose of causes is their legal effects, so the oath should deny the effect. For example, in a claim of sale, the defendant swears 'there is no current sale between us' (in case the sale was revoked), not 'I did not sell' which could be false if a sale occurred and was later canceled. Swearing on the effect protects the defendant from being forced to confess a past act that no longer produces liability, while still protecting the plaintiff's right because the plaintiff's real goal is the current ruling (e.g., ownership or debt). The text gives specific examples: for marriage, the oath is 'she is not irrevocably divorced'; for revocable divorce, 'she is not separate in the marriage'; for usurpation, 'no current usurpation exists'; for divorce, 'no current divorce exists'. If the defendant swore on the cause and was lying, he would lose the case; swearing on the effect allows him to be truthful if the cause has been raised. This is a form of legal stratagem (hila) to avoid injustice. The principle is general: wherever the cause can be nullified by a subsequent act, the oath is on the result.

Key Terms
الحاصل (al-hāṣil): The resulting legal effect or current status after considering any subsequent events that may have altered the original cause.
السبب (al-sabab): The original cause or event (e.g., contract of sale, marriage, act of usurpation, pronouncement of divorce) that is the basis of the claim.
الإقالة (al-iqālah): The mutual cancellation of a sale contract, which nullifies the original sale and its effects.
الرافع (al-rāfiʿ): The event that removes or nullifies the original cause (e.g., revocation, return of property, reconciliation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a claim of sale where the defendant denies, what should he swear according to the Hanafi school?
A) 'By Allah, I did not sell the item'
B) 'By Allah, there is no current sale between us'
C) 'By Allah, I do not owe the money'
D) 'By Allah, I did not buy the item'

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when the cause can be nullified, the defendant swears on the current legal effect (al-hāsil) to avoid perjury and balance the rights of both parties.

The Four Types of Division (Qismah) in Hanafi Law
Enumeration of four types of division with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four types of division (qismah) in Hanafi law and state the method used by Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn for each type.

Core Idea
Division of claims can be by 'awl or munaaza'ah; Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn disagree on which method applies in seven specific cases.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq classifies scenarios where multiple claimants must divide a single asset or compensation. The author enumerates four categories of division (qismah), specifying for each whether the division follows the method of 'awl (proportional reduction) or munaaza'ah (first-come-first-served). Notably, in the third and fourth categories, Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (the Sahibayn) differ on the appropriate method. Understanding this classification helps you predict the distribution outcome in cases involving inheritance, debts, bequests, slave-related torts, and unauthorized sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two division methods
Before examining the categories, know the two methods: 'awl (proportional reduction of shares when the total exceeds the fund) and munaaza'ah (the first claimant to seize the asset gets priority). The passage assumes you are familiar with these terms from earlier lessons.
If needed, review the definitions of 'awl and munaaza'ah in your fiqh glossary.
2
List the four categories
Read the passage and note the four headings: (1) cases divided by 'awl unanimously, (2) cases divided by munaaza'ah unanimously, (3) cases where Abu Hanifa uses munaaza'ah and the Sahibayn use 'awl, (4) cases where Abu Hanifa uses 'awl and the Sahibayn use munaaza'ah.
3
Tally the cases in each category
For categories 1 and 2, identify the number of cases: category 1 has eight cases (inheritance, debts, etc.), category 2 has one case (the fuduli slave sale). For categories 3 and 4, note that each contains a specific number of cases: three for category 3, five for category 4.
Memorizing the count helps in quick identification during exams.
4
Analyze the dispute in categories 3 and 4
For category 3, Abu Hanifa uses munaaza'ah while the Sahibayn use 'awl; for category 4, Abu Hanifa uses 'awl while the Sahibayn use munaaza'ah. Understand the practical implication: under 'awl, all claimants receive a proportionally reduced share; under munaaza'ah, the first to act (e.g., the first buyer) gets priority.
The dispute is rooted in different interpretations of how to handle conflicting claims when the fund is insufficient.
5
Apply the classification to a sample case
Take the fifth case of category 4: an umm walad kills her master and a stranger intentionally. The victim's heirs and the master's heirs have claims. According to Abu Hanifa, the division is by 'awl (proportional reduction); according to the Sahibayn, it is by munaaza'ah (priority to the first claimant). Trace how the outcome would differ.
This step bridges theory to practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن أنواع القسمة أربعة أحدها ما يقسم بطريق العول إجماعاً، وهي ثمان مسائل: الميراث، والديون، والوصية بما دون الثلث إذا اجتمعت وزادت على الثلث والمحاباة والدراهم المرسلة والسعاية، والعبد إذا قلع عين رجل، وقتل آخر خطأ، فدفع بهما، والمدبر إذا جنى على هذا الوجه فدفعت قيمته بهما، والثاني: ما يقسم بطريق المنازعة إجماعاً، وهي مسألة واحدة فضولي باع عبداً لغيره من رجل، وفضولي آخر باع فأجاز المولى البيعين، فاختار المشتريان الأخذ يكون لمشتري الكل ثلاثة أرباعه والمشتري النصف الربع بطريق المنازعة، والثالث ما يقسم بطريق المنازعة عند أبي حنيفة وعندهما بطريق العول، وهي ثلاث مسائل: إحداها: مسألة الكتاب والثانية: إذا أوصى رجل بجميع ماله وأجازه الورثة والثالثة إذا أوصى لرجل بعبد بعينه، ولآخر بنصف ذلك العبد، وليس له مال غيره. والرابع ما يقسم بطريق العول عند أبي حنيفة وعندهما بطريق المنازعة، وهي خمس مسائل: إحداها: عبد مأذون له في التجارة بين رجلين، أدانه أحدهما مائة وأدانه أجنبي مائة فدين المولى يسقط نصفه؛ لاستحالة الدين على عبده ويثبت نصف الدين في نصيب شريكه، فإذا بيع بالدين يقسمان الثمن على الخلاف الذي ذكرناه، والثانية: إذا أدانه أجنبيان أحدهما مائة والآخر مائتين، يقسمان ثمنه على هذا، والثالثة: عبد قتل رجلاً خطأ، وآخر عمداً، وللمقتول عمداً وليان فعفا أحدهما، فدفع بهما، كان بينهما على الخلاف، والرابعة: لو كان العبد الجاني مدبراً على هذا الوجه، فدفعت قيمته كانت بينهما كذلك، والخامسة أم ولد قتلت مولاها، وأجنبياً عمداً، ولكل واحد منهما وليّان، فعفا أحد وليي كل واحد منهما على التعاقب، فإنها تسعى في ثلاثة أرباع قيمتها، فتقسم على هذا الخلاف . (عيني، فتح) أيديهما: أي في أيدي مدعي النصف ومدعي الكل. (ط) وجوب
Know that the types of division are four. First: what is divided by way of 'awl unanimously, and these are eight cases: inheritance, debts, bequest of less than a third when they combine and exceed the third, favoritism (muhaabah), unrestricted dirhams, and compensation (sa'yah), and the slave who plucks out the eye of one man and kills another by mistake, so he is given in compensation for both, and the mudabbir who commits an offense in this manner so his value is given in compensation for both. Second: what is divided by way of munaaza'ah unanimously, and it is one case: a fuduli sells a slave belonging to another to a man, and another fuduli sells to another, and the master ratifies both sales, and the buyers choose to take; then the buyer of the whole gets three-quarters and the buyer of half gets one-quarter by way of munaaza'ah. Third: what is divided by way of munaaza'ah according to Abu Hanifa and by way of 'awl according to the two (Sahibayn), and these are three cases: one is the case of the book (the one we just discussed), the second is when a man bequeaths all his property and the heirs approve, the third is when a man bequeaths to a man a specific slave and to another half of that slave, and he has no other property. Fourth: what is divided by way of 'awl according to Abu Hanifa and by way of munaaza'ah according to the two, and these are five cases: one: a slave licensed to trade belonging to two men, one of them lends him one hundred and a stranger lends him one hundred; then the debt of the master drops by half because the debt cannot be owed to himself, and half of the debt is established in the share of his partner. So when the slave is sold for the debt, they divide the price according to the dispute we mentioned. Second: if two strangers lend to him, one one hundred and the other two hundred, they divide his price accordingly. Third: a slave kills one man by mistake and another intentionally, and the intentionally killed has two heirs, and one of them pardons, so he is given for both; it is between them according to the dispute. Fourth: if the offending slave is a mudabbir in this manner, then his value is given; it is between them likewise. Fifth: an umm walad kills her master and a stranger intentionally, and each has two heirs, and one heir of each pardons in succession; then she works for three-quarters of her value, and it is divided according to this dispute. (Ayni, Fath) 'Aydihima: meaning in the hands of the claimant of half and the claimant of the whole. (T) Wujub.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of division methods (qismah) in Hanafi jurisprudence. It categorizes scenarios where property or compensation must be divided among multiple claimants into four types. The first type is divided unanimously by 'awl: these are eight cases including inheritance, debts, and multiple bequests exceeding one-third. The second type is divided unanimously by munaaza'ah: only one case, involving a fuduli (unauthorized agent) selling a slave to two buyers, with the master ratifying. Here the buyer of the whole (the first buyer) gets three-quarters, and the buyer of half gets one-quarter, following the munaaza'ah method explained earlier. The third type is where Abu Hanifa uses munaaza'ah and the Sahibayn use 'awl: three cases, including the main case of the book (the house dispute), a bequest of all property with heir approval, and a specific slave bequeathed to one person and half to another. The fourth type is where Abu Hanifa uses 'awl and the Sahibayn use munaaza'ah: five cases involving a licensed slave, a slave committing homicide, a mudabbir slave, and an umm walad who kills her master and a stranger. The commentary clarifies that the opinions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ in the third and fourth categories.

Key Terms
العبد المأذون (al-'abd al-ma'dhun): A slave who is permitted by his master to engage in trade.
المدبر (al-mudabbir): A slave who is promised freedom after the master's death (tadbir).
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many cases fall under the fourth type of division (where Abu Hanifa uses 'awl and the Sahibayn use munaaza'ah)?
A) One
B) Three
C) Five
D) Eight

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, division (qismah) can be by 'awl (proportional reduction) or munaaza'ah (priority), and the method is not always unanimous—especially in cases involving slave-related transactions and torts, where Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn disagree.

Garment Dispute: Half for Each
Expansion of matn ruling on garment division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a garment dispute when each claimant holds a part, and distinguish when one claimant's hold is invalid (e.g., fringe).

Core Idea
Preference in possession disputes is by strength of hand, not by quantity held.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq expands the matn ruling that when two persons dispute over a garment and each has a part in hand, it is divided equally. The key principle is that preference is based on strength (quwwa) of possession, not on the amount held. We will explore the reasoning and exceptions, including the fringe case and the shirt analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Two men each have a part of the same garment in their hand. They both claim the entire garment. What is the ruling?
The garment is in both hands simultaneously.
2
Apply the principle of tarjih bil-quwwa
The Hanafi position: Since each hand is established on the garment, the possession is equal in strength. Quantity (one holds more fabric) does not create a preference. Therefore, divide equally.
Strength (quwwa) is parity of possession, not amount.
3
Distinguish the fringe case and the shirt analogy
If one claimant holds only the fringe (هدبة), which is not woven into the garment, his hand holds nothing of the garment itself. Thus, he has no valid possession and does not compete. In the shirt case, the increase (extra fabric on the wearer) is not of the same type as the evidence (use), so the wearer's hand is not equivalent to the holder's hand.
The fringe is not considered part of the garment for possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نصف: أي إذا كان الثوب في يد رجل وطرفه في يد رجل آخر، وتنازعا فيه، نصف الثوب بينهما؛ لأن يد كل واحد منهما ثابت فيه، ولا عبرة بالكثرة في يد أحدهما؛ لأن الترجيح بالقوة لا بالكثرة، بخلاف ما إذا كان في يد الآخر الهدبة؛ إذ هي غير منسوجة، فلم يكن في يده شيء من الثوب، فلا يزاحم الآخر. والفرق بين هذا وبين مسألة القميص: أن الزيادة فيها ليس من جنس الحجة، فإن الحجة الاستعمال، فكان اليد، والزيادة هي اللابس أحق فيها من الآخذ وههنا الزيادة من جنس الحجة، فإن كل واحد منهما مستمسك باليد إلا أن أحدهما أكثر استمساكاً، ومثل ذلك لا يوجب الرجحان ككثرة الشهود. (عيني، فتح، عناية)
Half: That is, if a garment is in the hand of a man and its edge is in the hand of another man, and they dispute over it, half of the garment is between them; because the hand of each is established over it, and no consideration is given to the greater amount in the hand of one of them; because preference is by strength not by abundance, contrary to the case where the fringe is in the hand of the other; since it is not woven, so there is nothing of the garment in his hand, so he does not compete with the other. And the difference between this and the case of the shirt: that the increase in that case is not of the same type as the evidence, for the evidence is use, so the hand was [the basis], and the increase is that the wearer has more right to it than the taker, whereas here the increase is of the same type as the evidence, for each of them is grasping by hand except that one has more grasp, and that does not cause preponderance like the abundance of witnesses.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage expands the matn ruling that when two persons dispute over a garment and each has a part in hand, the garment is divided equally. The reason is that both hands are established on the garment, and the amount each holds does not matter because preference is based on strength of possession, not quantity. If one only holds the fringe (هدبة) which is not woven into the garment, he has no part of the garment in his hand, so the other is preferred. The distinction is made with the case of a shirt where the increase (the extra fabric covering the body) is not from the same type as the proof (the use of the garment), but here both are grasping by hand, so the one with more grasp does not get preference.

Key Terms
يد (yad): Physical possession or control; in this context, the part of the garment held by each claimant.
ترجيح بالقوة (tarjīḥ bil-quwwah): Preference based on the strength of possession, not quantity held.
هدبة (hudba): Fringe; non-woven threads attached to the edge of a garment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Divided equally between them
B) Given entirely to the one holding the main body
C) Given entirely to the one holding the fringe
D) The fringe is considered separately, the rest is divided

Key Takeaway
In a Hanafi garment dispute, equal division applies only when both parties have a valid hand on the garment itself (woven fabric); holding a non-woven fringe does not constitute valid possession.

Child's Claim of Freedom or Slavery
Ruling on a discerning child's claim of freedom or slavery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a child's claim of freedom or slavery and apply the distinction between a discerning and non-discerning child.

Core Idea
A discerning child is in his own hand; his word on freedom holds, but his admission of slavery shifts possession.

This lesson addresses a specific legal scenario: a child whose status (free or slave) is disputed. The Hanafi school gives weight to the child's own statement if he is discerning, but treats a non-discerning child as property of the possessor. We will examine the reasoning behind these rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the child's capacity
First, determine whether the child is discerning (mu'abbar 'an nafsihi) or not. This is the key condition that determines the ruling.
Discerning means the child understands what he says and what is said to him.
2
Analyze the child's claim
If the child is discerning and claims freedom, his statement is accepted because he is in his own hand. If he admits being a slave of another, the holder's claim prevails.
The child's admission of slavery to another shows he has no hand over himself.
3
Apply the ruling for non-discerning child
If the child cannot express himself, he is like a commodity; the holder's claim to ownership is accepted without opposition.
Later claims of freedom by the child after growing up require evidence.
4
Understand the reasoning (talil)
The Hanafi scholars reason that a free person is in his own hand, and a child's admission of slavery is not harmful because he can later claim freedom.
This addresses the objection that admissions harmful to a minor are invalid.
5
Note cross-madhhab differences
Other schools may restrict a child's ability to admit slavery more strictly, but the Hanafi position is practical.
The difference arises from how each school views the child's capacity and harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا صبي يعبر عن نفسه، فقال: أنا حر فالقول له، وإن قال: أنا عبد لفلان غير ذي اليد، وصاحب اليد يدعي أنه عبده، فهو عبد لمن هو في يده. وإن كان الصبي لا يعبر عن نفسه، فهو عبد لمن هو في يده. (عيني) نفس: يعقل ما يقول أو يقال. فالقول له: لأنه في يد نفسه فلا تقبل دعوى أحد عليه أنه عبده عند إنكاره إلا ببينة كالبالغ. وفي كلام المصنف إدخال الفاء في خبر المبتدأ الذي ليس هو من صيغ العموم، وهو مما منعه الجمهور وأجازه الأخفش، وأجيب بأن المبتدأ هنا نكرة موصوفة بجملة وهي من صيغ العموم. (عيني، فتح) فهو عبد إلخ: أي في الصورتين الأولى: إذا قال الصبي الذي يعبر عن نفسه: أنا عبد لفلان غير ذي اليد، والذي أنه هو في يده يدعي عبده، فهو عبد لمن هو في يده؛ لأنه أقر أنه لا يد له حيث أقر على نفسه بالرق، لا يقال: الإقرار بالرق من المضار، فلا يعتبر من الصبي، فكيف يصح إقراره به؛ لأنا نقول: الرق لم يثبت بقول الصبي، بل بدعوى ذي اليد؛ لعدم المعارض بدعوى الحرية؛ لأنه لما صار في يد المدعي بقي كالقماش في يده، فيقبل إقراره عليه، ولا نسلم أن الإقرار بالرق من المضار؛ لأنه يمكنه التدارك بعده بدعوى الحرية؛ إذ التناقض فيه لا يمنع صحة الدعوى، بخلاف الإقرار بالدين، وأما في الصورة الثانية وهو ما إذا كان الصبي لا يعبر عن نفسه، فهو عبد لمن في يده؛ لأنه بمنزلة المتاع، فيكون ملكاً لمن هو في يده إن ادعاه ذو اليد؛ لعدم المعارض، ولو كبر الصبي وادعى الحرية، لا يقبل قوله؛ لأنه ظهر عليه الرق في حال صغره، فلا ينتقض ذلك إلا بالبينة. (عيني، فتح)
No, a child who can express himself: if he says 'I am free', his statement is accepted; if he says 'I am a slave of so-and-so' other than the one holding him, and the holder claims him as his slave, then he is a slave of the one in whose hand he is. And if the child cannot express himself, then he is a slave of the one in whose hand he is. (Ayni) 'Nafs' means he understands what he says or what is said. So his statement is accepted: because he is in his own hand, so no one's claim of his being a slave upon his denial is accepted except with evidence like an adult. ... So he is a slave etc.: that is, in the first situation: if the child who can express himself says 'I am a slave of so-and-so' other than the holder, and the one in whose hand he is claims him as his slave, then he is a slave of the one in whose hand he is; because he has admitted that he has no hand [over himself] by admitting to slavery. ... In the second situation: if the child cannot express himself, then he is a slave of the one in whose hand he is; because he is like a commodity, so he becomes property of the one in whose hand he is if the holder claims him; due to lack of opposition. And if the child grows up and claims freedom, his statement is not accepted because his slavery was established in his childhood, so that is not overturned except by evidence.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage explains the matn ruling on a child who can express himself. If he says 'I am free', his word is taken because he is in his own custody. If he says 'I am a slave of someone else' (not the holder), and the actual holder claims him, he becomes the slave of the holder because his admission shows he has no ownership over himself. The child's admission of slavery is not considered harmful to him because he can later claim freedom, unlike admission of debt. If the child cannot express himself, he is considered a slave to the holder by default. Later, if he claims freedom as an adult, it is not accepted without evidence.

Key Terms
يعبر عن نفسه (yu'abbiro 'an nafsihi): A child who can clearly express himself and understand what is said.
يد نفسه (yad nafsihi): Being in one's own custody or control, as opposed to being in the hand of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a discerning child says 'I am the slave of Zaid' (who is not the current holder), and the current holder claims him, what is the ruling?
A) The child is declared free
B) The child becomes the slave of the current holder
C) The child becomes the slave of Zaid
D) The child's statement is ignored

Key Takeaway
A discerning child is in his own hand; his claim of freedom is accepted, but his admission of slavery to another shifts possession to the holder.
