Wall and Jutting Wood: Right of Placement
Ruling on beam owner's right and wall ownership via connection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rights of a beam owner to keep his beam in place and explain how continuous connection (ittisāl al-tarbīʿ) determines priority in wall ownership disputes.

Core Idea
A beam owner may keep his beam unless the wall's owner is proven; continuous interlocking bricks give priority in wall claims.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses two related property disputes: the right of a beam owner to have his beam remain on a neighbor's wall, and the more complex case of two individuals claiming a wall. The key distinction is between simple adjoining (mulāzaqa) and continuous interlocking (tarbīʿ), which serves as evidence of a single builder. Understanding these rules clarifies how Hanafi fiqh resolves ownership conflicts based on physical construction evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage first discusses the right of a beam owner, then a wall ownership dispute based on connection type.
2
Examine the beam owner's right
The beam owner may keep his beam unless the wall owner proves his ownership with evidence.
3
Analyze the wall dispute
When two claim a wall, the one with continuous connection (interlocking) is more entitled.
4
Define continuous connection
Tarbīʿ involves interlocking bricks or inserted wooden boards, indicating a single builder.
5
Distinguish simple adjoining
Mulāzaqa is not given weight because it does not prove a common builder.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ببعيد، ولصاحب الجذع أو الجذعين حق الوضع فلا يؤمر بالقلع؛ لأن الظاهر يصلح للدفع لا للاستحقاق، إلا إذا ثبت بالبينة أن الحائط لصاحب الجذوع، والثالثة: إذا كان لرجل حائط متصل ببنائه، فادعاه الآخر، فصاحب الاتصال أحق بالحائط من الآخر، والمراد بالاتصال مداخلة لبن جداره فيه ولبن هذا في جداره، ويسمى هذا الاتصال باتصال التربيع إذا كان الحائط من آجر، وإن كان من خشب فالتربيع أن يكون ساحة أحدهما مركبة في الأخرى، واحترز به عن اتصال الملازقة، فإنه غير معتبر، فاتصال التربيع لا يكون إلا عند البناء، فدل ذلك على أن بانيهما واحد في وقت واحد، فترجح به، ويكون أحق من غير. (عيني، فتح، عناية)
Far removed, and the owner of one or two beams has the right to place them, so he is not ordered to remove them; because the apparent [evidence] is for repelling, not for entitlement, unless it is proven by evidence that the wall belongs to the owner of the beams. And the third: if a man has a wall attached to his building, and another claims it, the one with continuity is more entitled to the wall than the other. What is meant by continuity is interlocking of the bricks of his wall into it and the bricks of this into his wall. This continuity is called 'continuous connection' if the wall is of brick, and if it is of wood, then the connection is that the board of one is inserted into the other. This excludes simple adjoining connection, which is not considered. Continuous connection only occurs during construction, so it indicates that the builder of both is one and the same at one time, so he is preferred and is more entitled than the other. (Ayni, Fath, Inaya)
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage discusses the right of an owner who has placed a beam or beams (جذع) on a neighbor's wall. He has the right to have them remain (وضع) but cannot be forced to remove them because the beam's presence is for the owner's benefit, not for claiming ownership of the wall. However, if the neighbor proves by evidence that the wall is his, then the beam may need to be removed. The third case is when two persons claim a wall: the one whose building has a 'continuous connection' (اتصال التربيع) with the wall is preferred over someone who does not. Continuous connection means the bricks are interlocked, indicating both were built together, so the builder is the owner. Simple adjoining without interlocking is not enough.

Key Terms
اتصال التربيع (ittisāl al-tarbīʿ): Continuous connection through interlocking bricks or wood, indicating a single builder.
اتصال الملازقة (ittisāl al-mulāzaqa): Simple adjoining without interlocking, not considered proof of ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling regarding a beam placed on a neighbor's wall?
A) The beam owner must remove it immediately.
B) The beam owner has the right to keep it unless the wall owner proves ownership.
C) The beam owner automatically gains partial ownership of the wall.
D) The beam must be removed if it causes any shade or inconvenience.

Key Takeaway
Continuous connection through interlocking masonry or wood provides a strong presumption of common ownership, while simple placing of beams does not grant ownership.

Division of a Shared Courtyard by Heads (Qismah 'ala al-Ru'us)
Ruling on dividing a shared courtyard when owners have unequal room counts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of equal division (by heads) to shared amenities and distinguish it from proportional division for water rights.

Core Idea
For shared amenities like a courtyard, division is by heads (equal), not by property size.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi ruling on how to divide a common courtyard when owners have unequal shares in the building. The key insight is that the need for common amenities—like passage, ablution, and storage—is equal for all residents, regardless of how many rooms they own. Therefore, the courtyard is divided equally, a principle that also applies to roads, pre-emption fees, and certain other shared obligations. We will explore the reasoning and contrast it with the proportional division used for water shares.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Two owners: one has ten rooms, the other one room. They disagree on how to divide the shared courtyard.
2
Apply the rule
The courtyard is divided equally (half each). Reason: both owners have equal need for common amenities like passage, ablution, etc.
This is division by heads (qismah 'ala al-ru'us).
3
Contrast with water share
For irrigation water, division is proportional to land area because the need for water scales with the size of land.
The illa (effective cause) differs: equal need vs. proportional need.
4
Generalize the principle
The rule of division by heads applies to: courtyard, pre-emption (shuf'ah), fee of the divider, unjust taxes, blood money, jettisoned cargo, and road. The underlying criterion is equal need.
Memorize these cases as mentioned in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عشرة أبيات من دار في يده وبيت في يد آخر، فالساحة نصفان. ادعى كل أرضا أنها في يده، ولبن أحدهما فيها أو بنى أو حفر فهي في يده كما لو برهن أنها في يده. أي إذا كانت عشرة أبيات من الدار في يد رجل وبيت واحد منها في يد رجل آخر، وتنازعا في ساحة، فالساحة نصفان بينهما؛ لأن كلاً الملاك يحتاج إليها للاستعمال في أنواع المرافق من المرور فيها والتوضئ وكسر الحطب ووضع الأمتعة ونحو ذلك، ذلك سواء فتنصف بينهما، فصار نظير الطريق؛ لأن الترجيح بالقوة لا بالكثرة، بخلاف ما إذا تنازعا في الشرب، حيث يقسم بينهما على قدر أراضيهما؛ لأن الاحتياج إليه لأجل السقي، فعند كثرة الأراضي تكثر الحاجة. [ رمز الحقائق: ۲۰۹/۲۸] واعلم أن القسمة على الرؤوس في الساحة والشفعة وأجرة القسام والنوائب المأخوذة ظلما والعاقلة وما يرمى من المركب خوف الغرق والطريق. (فتح)
Ten rooms of a house in his hand and one room in the hand of another, then the courtyard is half and half. If each claims land that it is in his hand, and one has bricks in it, or built, or dug, then it is in his hand, as if he provided evidence that it is in his hand. That is, if ten rooms of the house are in the hand of a man and one room in the hand of another man, and they dispute about the courtyard, then the courtyard is half between them; because both owners need it for use in various amenities: passing through, performing ablution, breaking firewood, placing luggage, etc., that is equal, so it is divided between them. So it is like the case of a road; because preference is by strength not by abundance, contrary to the case of water share, where it is divided according to the size of their lands; because the need for it is for irrigation, so when land is abundant, the need is greater. And know that division by heads applies in courtyard, pre-emption, fee of the divider, taxes taken unjustly, blood money, items thrown overboard fearing drowning, and road. (Fath)
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute over a shared courtyard. One person owns ten rooms, the other only one room in the same building. They need the courtyard for common uses like walking, washing, etc. These needs are equal regardless of the number of rooms, so the courtyard is divided equally (by heads, not by the number of rooms). This is analogous to a shared road where everyone has equal access. In contrast, if they dispute over water for irrigation, the division is proportional to land size because the need is directly related to agricultural area. The commentator notes that division by heads applies in several cases: courtyard, pre-emption (shuf'ah), fee of the divider, unjust taxes, blood money, jettisoning cargo, and public road.

Key Terms
قسمة على الرؤوس (qismah 'alā al-ru'ūs): Equal division per person, regardless of property share.
ساحة (sāḥah): Courtyard; open space within a building shared by residents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How is a courtyard divided when one owner has ten rooms and another has one room?
A) In proportion to the number of rooms
B) Equally between them
C) The one with ten rooms gets ten times more
D) The courtyard is not divided

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, shared amenities like courtyards are divided equally by heads, not proportionally to property size, because the need for such amenities is equal for all owners.

Proof of Land Possession Through Dispositive Acts
Ruling with intra-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for establishing possession of land through physical actions and explain the intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding dividing the land.

Core Idea
Land possession is proven not by mere claim but by concrete acts like building or digging.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a dispute where two parties each claim the same piece of land. Since land is immovable and its possession is not directly observable, the Hanafi school requires external evidence. The passage explains that acts such as laying bricks, building, or digging a well serve as proof of possession. It also discusses what happens when both parties present such evidence, and notes a difference between Abu Hanifa and his two students (the Sahibayn).

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal problem
Two parties each allege possession of the same land. The judge cannot rely on mere claims because land possession is not directly observable.
2
Recognize the evidentiary solution
Physical acts such as laying bricks (talbīn), building (binā'), or digging a well (ḥafr) serve as evidence of possession because they are observable manifestations of control.
These acts are qarīnah (circumstantial evidence) accepted in Hanafi law.
3
Apply when both parties have evidence
If both parties present evidence of such acts, the judge rules that the land is in both their hands (co-possession).
The acts need not be simultaneous or on the entire land; any part suffices to show hand.
4
Understand the post-judgment dispute
After co-possession is established, if they request division, Abu Hanifa says division requires proof of ownership, while the two students say division is allowed based on possession evidence.
The dispute hinges on whether possession alone (without ownership) justifies partitioning.
5
Analvze the objection and response
An objection: If one party is not established as an opponent, how can his evidence be heard? The response: Disputing the hand makes him an opponent, so his evidence is acceptable.
This clarifies that the opponent status is based on the dispute itself, not on prior proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ادعى كل أرضا أنها في يده، ولبن أحدهما فيها أو بنى أو حفر فهي في يده كما لو برهن أنها في يده. أي كل واحد من الاثنين. ولبن: بتشديد الباء أي ضرب لبناً وهو الطوب الذي. (عيني) أو بنى: أي بني فيها أحدهما بناء. (عيني) أو حفر أي حفر أحدهما فيها بئراً. (عيني) فهي في يده لأن اليد في الأرض غير مشاهدة، فلا تثبت عند القاضي بمجرد دعواهما أنها يده، فلابد من إقامة البيئة أو من تصرف فيها كالتلبين والبناء وحفر البئر؛ لأن هذه الأشياء دليل على أنها في يده، ثم إن تصادقا أنها في أيديهما أو في يد أحدهما لم يقض بلا بينة؛ لاحتمال تواضعهما على ذلك. (عيني، فتح) كما لو برهن: [ أي أقام البينة على أنها إلخ (عيني)] فإنه يقضى له باليد فيها، ويكون الآخر خارجاً، وإن برهنا على أنها في أيديهما قضي بها لهما، فإن طلبا القسمة لم تقسم بينهما ما لم يقيما البينة على الملك، قيل: هذا قول أبي حنيفة، وعندهما تقسم رمز الحقائق: ۲۱۰/۲] فإن قيل: البينة تقام على خصم وحيث لم يثبت أنها في يد الآخر فليس بخصم أجيب بأنه خصم باعتبار منازعة في اليد، ومن كان خصماً لغيره باعتبار منازعة في شيء شرعا، كانت بينته مقبولة. (عناية)
If each claims land that it is in his hand, and one has bricks in it, or built, or dug, then it is in his hand, as if he provided evidence that it is in his hand. That is, each of the two. And 'bricks' means he made bricks, which are clay blocks. Or 'built' means one built a structure in it. Or 'dug' means one dug a well in it. So it is in his hand because the hand in land is not observable, so it is not established before the judge merely by their claim that it is his hand, so it is necessary to present evidence or to have a dispositive act such as brick-making, building, or digging a well; because these things are evidence that it is in his hand. Then if they both agree that it is in their hands or in the hand of one of them, the judge does not decide without evidence; because of the possibility that they conspired on that. (Ayni, Fath) 'As if he provided evidence': that is, he presented evidence that it is in his hand. Then the judge rules for him regarding the hand over it, and the other is excluded. If both provide evidence that it is in their hands, the judge rules that it is for both. If they request division, it is not divided between them as long as they do not provide evidence of ownership. It is said: this is the view of Abu Hanifa, while his two students say it is divided. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 210/2) If it is said: evidence is presented against an opponent, and since it is not established that it is in the hand of the other, he is not an opponent, the answer is that he is an opponent by virtue of disputing the hand, and whoever is an opponent to another by virtue of disputing something legally, his evidence is acceptable. (Inaya)
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute over land where both claim possession. Since physical possession of land is not directly visible, the judge requires some evidence of possession, such as brick-laying, building, or digging. These actions are considered dispositive acts that indicate control. If both parties claim to have done such acts, the judge may rule for both. However, if they both claim possession without evidence, no ruling is made. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa says that if both provide evidence of possession, the land is not divided until they also prove ownership, while the two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it is divided. The commentator also addresses an objection: if the opponent is not established as an opponent, how can his evidence be considered? The response is that merely disputing the hand makes him an opponent, so his evidence is acceptable.

Key Terms
يد في الأرض غير مشاهدة (yad fī al-arḍ ghayr mushāhadah): Possession of land is not physically observable unlike moveable property.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what type of act can prove possession of land?
A) Written contract
B) Building a structure
C) Verbal testimony of two witnesses
D) Neighbor's agreement

Key Takeaway
Land possession is proven by observable physical acts such as building or digging, not by mere claim.

8 min
Istihsan Overrides Qiyas in Seller's Lineage Claim
Resolving the dispute between Zufar and the majority via istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school accepts the seller's claim by istihsan despite the apparent contradiction with qiyas.

Core Idea
Certainty of conception in ownership and the presumption of legitimacy override the apparent contradiction of selling.

This lesson explores a classic intra-Hanafi dispute where qiyas would reject a seller's paternity claim due to conflicting actions, but istihsan prevails due to certainty about conception. You will see how the majority (Abu Hanifa and others) prioritize factual certainty over logical inconsistency, a hallmark of Hanafi methodology.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the majority (including Abu Hanifa) accept the seller's claim by istihsan, making the child free and the slave an umm walad, and rescind the sale.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
Zufar and the three (including Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in one narration) argue by qiyas that the claim is invalid because selling the slave implies the seller did not consider her his umm walad.
The opposing dalil is the apparent contradiction.
3
Follow the opponent's dalil
Understand the qiyas: if you sell something, it contradicts a claim of ownership that would prevent sale. Here, selling the pregnant slave suggests the child is not his, so the claim is contradictory and should be rejected.
Qiyas focuses on external consistency.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response via istihsan
The majority argue that we are certain conception occurred in his ownership (because birth within six months), and the default is legitimacy. This certainty (qat') overrides the probabilistic indication (zann) of contradiction, so the claim is accepted.
Istihsan gives weight to al-baqi' al-azali (original certainty).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If the claim is accepted: child is free, mother becomes umm walad, buyer gets refund. If rejected: both remain slaves of the buyer.
The practical consequence is significant for the parties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي أم ولده: أي إذا ثبت نسب الولد من البائع فالولد ابنه، والأمة المبيعة أم ولد البائع استحساناً، والقياس: أن تكون هذه الدعوى باطلة؛ لأن إقدامه على البيع دليل على أن الحمل ليس منه والولد عبد له، فكان في دعواه متناقضاً، فلا تسمع دعواه، فإذا لم تصح الدعوى لم يثبت النسب؛ إذ لا نسب في الجارية بدون الدعوى، وهو قول زفر والثلاثة، وجه الاستحسان أنا تيقنا باتصال العلوق في ملكه، وذلك شهادة ظاهرة على كونه منه؛ لأن الظاهر عدم الزنا، فنزل ذلك منزلة البينة في إبطال حق الغير عنها وعن ولدها، فتصح دعواه مستنداً إلى وقت العلوق، فيفسخ البيع؛ لأنه قد باع أم ولده ويرد الثمن أي ثمن المبيعة إلى المشتري؛ لأنه مقتضى الفسخ في العقد.
And she is his umm walad: i.e., when the lineage of the child is established from the seller, the child is his son, and the sold female slave becomes the seller's umm walad by istihsan. The qiyas is that this claim should be invalid because his proceeding to sell indicates that the pregnancy is not from him and the child is his slave, so in his claim he is contradictory, thus his claim is not heard. If the claim is not valid, lineage is not established because there is no lineage for the female slave without a claim. This is the view of Zufar and the three. The justification of istihsan is that we are certain that conception occurred in his ownership, and that is a clear testimony that it is from him, because the apparent state is absence of zina. So this is given the status of evidence (bayyinah) in abrogating the right of others concerning her and her child. So his claim is valid based on the time of conception, so the sale is rescinded because he sold his umm walad, and the price (thaman) is returned to the buyer, because that is the requirement of rescission of the contract.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the seller's claim. Zufar and the three imams (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and others) argue by qiyas that the seller's claim should be invalid because his act of selling contradicts his claim of paternity; selling the slave implies she is not his umm walad. However, the Hanafi majority (Abu Hanifa and others) adopt istihsan: since we know with certainty that conception occurred in his ownership (because birth within six months confirms pregnancy at the time of sale), and the default assumption is legitimacy (absence of zina), this certainty overrides the apparent contradiction. Thus, the claim is accepted, the sale is rescinded, and the price is returned. This exemplifies the Hanafi principle of istihsan overriding qiyas when a stronger indication is present.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): A juristic preference that overrides qiyas in favor of a more equitable result, used here to validate the seller's claim despite apparent contradiction.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning that would invalidate the claim because selling implies the child is not from the seller.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school accept the seller's claim by istihsan despite the apparent contradiction?
A) Because the seller repented
B) Because conception is certain in his ownership and default is legitimacy
C) Because the buyer confirmed the claim
D) Because the child is less than six months old

Key Takeaway
Certainty (qat') of conception in ownership and the presumption of legitimacy allow istihsan to override the apparent contradiction of selling.

Claiming Lineage of Twins Voids Sale and Freeing
Ruling with analogy on twins' lineage and voiding of freedom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on claiming lineage of twins and explain why the buyer's freeing is voided when the seller claims the other twin.

Core Idea
Claiming one twin establishes both, and freeing one is voided because both must share the same status.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a man's slave-girl gives birth to twins; he sells one, the buyer frees him, and later the seller claims the other twin as his son. According to the Hanafi school, the claim establishes both twins as free, retroactively voiding the freeing of the sold twin. The reasoning is based on the physical reality that twins originate from a single conception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
State the facts: a man sells one twin to a buyer who frees him; later the seller claims the other twin as his son. The ruling: the buyer's freeing is voided, and both twins are established as the seller's sons and free.
2
Understand the rational basis (dalil aqli)
Twins are created from one sperm (ماء واحد); therefore, the lineage of one necessarily establishes the lineage of the other. This is known from the common interval of less than six months between births.
The passage uses qiyas shabah (analogy of resemblance) to the case of a single child.
3
Apply the principle to the freeing
Once the seller claims the unsold twin, both become free by origin. It is impossible for one twin to be free and the other a slave. Therefore, the freeing of the sold twin (which made him a slave) is voided retroactively.
4
Contrast with a single child
If there was only one child sold and freed, a later claim by the seller does not void the freeing because that would defeat the purpose of the transaction. The seller's right is weaker than the reality of the freeing.
The distinction is based on the principle that many things are established implicitly even if not primarily.
5
Note the practical consequence
The buyer loses his slave and must return him? The passage does not discuss remedy, but the freeing is void, so the slave returns to being free and the buyer loses his property right.
This ruling may differ in other madhhabs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتح التوأمين وهما ولدان بين ولادتيهما أقل من ستة أشهر. (ط) ثبت نسبهما منه لأنهما خلقا من ماء ،واحد فثبوت نسب أحدهما يستلزم ثبوت نسب الآخر، وإنما يعرف أنهما توأمان إذا كان بين ولادتهما أقل من ستة أشهر، وإن كان بينهما ستة أشهر فصاعداً، فليسا بتوأمين؛ لأن أقل مدة الحمل ستة أشهر ، فإذا أتت بولد ثم بولد آخر لأقل من ستة أشهر يعلم بالضرورة أنهما من ماء واحد. رمز الحقائق: ۲۱۲/۲] (فتح) وأعتقه المشتري: ثم ادعى البائع بعده أنه ابنه ثبت نسبهما منه. (عيني) بطل عتق المشتري: أي إذا ولدت أمة رجل توأمين ثم باع الرجل أحدهما وأعتقه المشتري، ثم ادعى البائع نسياً الآخر، بطل عتق المشتري وثبت نسبهما منه، وجه بطلان العتق: أن دعوى البائع بعده صحت في الذي لم يبعه، ولمن ضرورة ذلك ثبوت نسب الآخر؛ لأنهما من ماء ،واحد فيلزم منه بطلان عتق المشتري؛ لكونهما حري الأصل؛ إذ يستحيل أن يكون أحدهما حرّ الأصل، والآخر ،رقيقاً، وهما من ماء واحد بخلاف ما إذا كان الولد واحداً، فباعه المالك وأعتقه المشتري، حيث لا يبطل فيه إعتاق المشتري، بدعوى البائع نسبه؛ لأن العتق لو بطل فيه بطل المقصود؛ للأجل حق الدعوى للبائع، وإنه لا يجوز؛ لأن عتق المشتري حقيقة وحق البائع أدنى من الحقيقة فلا يعارضه فكيف يرفعه، وهنا تثبت الحرية في الذي لم يبعه، ثم تتعدى إلى الآخر ضمناً وتبعاً، وكم من شيء يثبت ضمناً وإن لم يثبت مقصوداً.
Commentary on twins: They are two children between whose births less than six months have passed. His lineage from him is established for both because they are created from one drop of water. The establishment of the lineage of one necessitates the establishment of the other. Twins are known only if the interval between births is less than six months. If it is six months or more, they are not twins because the minimum duration of pregnancy is six months. So if she gives birth to a child, then another child after less than six months, it is known necessarily that they are from one drop. ... He buys one and frees him: then the seller claims after that that he is his son, the lineage of both is established from him. The freeing of the buyer is void: that is, if a man's slave-girl gives birth to twins, then the man sells one of them and the buyer frees him, then the seller claims the lineage of the other, the buyer's freeing is void and the lineage of both is established from him. The reason for the voidance is that the seller's claim after that is valid for the one he did not sell, and from that necessity the lineage of the other is established because they are from one drop. Consequently, the buyer's freeing is void because both are originally free; it is impossible that one is originally free and the other a slave while they are from one drop. Unlike if there was a single child, and the owner sold him and the buyer freed him, the buyer's freeing is not voided by the seller's claim of lineage, because if it were voided, the purpose would be defeated. Because the claim of the seller is a right, and it is not permissible to oppose the reality of the buyer's freeing. Here freedom is established for the one not sold, then it extends to the other implicitly and as a consequence. Many things are established implicitly even if not primarily.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on claiming lineage of twins. If a man claims paternity of one twin, both are established as his because they originate from a single conception. The passage then discusses a scenario: a man's slave-girl gives birth to twins; he sells one of them and the buyer frees him. Later, the seller claims the other twin as his son. This results in the buyer's freeing being voided because both twins are from the same sperm, so they must share the same status. The commentary contrasts this with the case of a single child: if a seller sells his son and the buyer frees him, the seller's later claim of paternity does not void the freeing; otherwise, the purpose of the sale and freeing would be defeated. The key principle is that the freedom of one twin necessarily entails the freedom of the other due to their origin.

Key Terms
توأمان (taw'amān): Twins: two children born from the same conception, with less than six months between their births.
حرّ الأصل (hurr al-aṣl): Originally free: a person who is born free, not a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The freeing stands; only the unsold twin becomes free.
B) The freeing is voided, and both twins are free.
C) The freeing is voided only if the seller claims the freed twin.
D) The seller's claim is rejected because he sold one.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling: claiming one twin establishes both as free from the father, retroactively voiding any freeing of the other twin.

Custodian's Invalid Claim of Paternity After Prior Acknowledgment
Ruling that custodian's claim of paternity is invalid if he previously acknowledged the boy as another's son.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a custodian's claim of paternity after a prior acknowledgment, and explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the effect of the absent person's denial.

Core Idea
A prior acknowledgment of another's paternity prevents a later claim of paternity, even if the acknowledgee denies it.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario: a person has a boy in his custody. He first states that the boy is the son of an absent individual. Later, he claims the boy is his own son. Does this claim hold any legal weight? The Hanafi school presents a nuanced discussion with a disagreement between the founder, Abu Hanifa, and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. We will explore the ruling, the proofs, and the practical outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the initial acknowledgment
Understand the facts: a person (dhū al-yad) has custody of a boy. He first declares the boy to be the son of an absent person (Zayd, for example). This is a binding acknowledgment (iqrār).
The acknowledgment is against the custodian's own interest—it establishes lineage for another.
2
Analyze the three possibilities regarding the absent person's response
The absent person may confirm, deny, or remain silent. In all cases, the custodian later claims the boy as his own. The ruling on the claim differs depending on the response and the scholar's view.
There is consensus that if the absent person confirms or remains silent, the claim is invalid.
3
Understand Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa holds that even if the absent person denies, the claim is invalid. His proof: the acknowledgment is a proof against the acknowledger that cannot be undone; it is like an admission of another's right. He uses the analogy of acknowledging the freedom of another's slave—the acknowledgment stands even if the owner denies, so the slave becomes free when the acknowledger later owns him.
This analogy shows that an iqrār remains binding on the declarant.
4
Understand the Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that the absent person's denial voids the acknowledgment, as if it never happened. Therefore, the custodian is free to claim the boy as his own son.
They view the denial as a rejection of the right, making the acknowledgment null.
5
Apply the tarjih and determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa. The claim is invalid, but a legal consequence follows: the boy is considered free (as a result of the claim being treated as a declaration of freedom in analogy to the slave case).
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقال: هو ابن فلان ثم قال: هو ابني، لم يكن ابنه، وإن جحد أن يكون ابنه، ولو كان صبي في يد رجل، فأقر ذو اليد أنه ابن فلان الغائب ثم قال: هو ابني، لم يكن ابنه في وقت من الأوقات، لا حالاً ولا مستقبلاً، أما حالاً فظاهر؛ لأن إقراره بنسب من الغير يمنع ثبوت نسبه منه بدعوته، وأما استقبالاً؛ فلأن الغائب لا يخلو حاله عن ثلاث إما أن يصدقه أو يكذبه، أو يسكت عن التصديق والتكذيب، ففي الوجه الأول والسكوت لا تصح دعوته بالاتفاق؛ لأنه لم يتصل بإقراره تكذيب من جهة المقر له فبقي إقراره، وفي الوجه الثاني أي إذا كذبه المقر له لم تصح دعوته عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا: تصح دعوة المقر بعد جحود المقرله أن يكون ابنه؛ لأن إقراره له بطل بجحود المقرله، فصار كأنه لم يقر وله: أن النسب مما لا يحتمل النقض بعد ثبوته والإقرار بمثله لا يرتد بالرد، فبقي في حق نفسه، فيمنع دعوته؛ لأنه حجة في حق نفسه، كمن أقر بحرية عبد الغير وكذبه المولى، فإنه يبقى في حق المقر حراً، ولا يرتد إقراره، حتى لو ملكه يوماً عتق؛ لإقراره بذلك، والحاصل أنه لا يثبت نسبه من المقر، ولكن يعتق عليه بدعوته. (عيني، فتح، عناية)
He said: "He is the son of so-and-so," then said: "He is my son," he is not his son, even if he denies that he is his son. If there is a boy in the hand of a man, and the custodian acknowledged that he is the son of the absent person, then said: "He is my son," he is not his son at any time, neither present nor future. As for the present, it is obvious because his acknowledgment of the lineage to another prevents the establishment of his lineage from him by his claim. As for the future, because the absent person's case is one of three: either he confirms him, or denies him, or remains silent. In the first case and silence, his claim is invalid by agreement because his acknowledgment has not been contradicted by the acknowledgee, so his acknowledgment remains. In the second case, i.e., if the acknowledgee denies him, his claim is invalid according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: The claim of the acknowledger is valid after the acknowledgee's denial that he is his son, because his acknowledgment for him is voided by the acknowledgee's denial, so it is as if he did not acknowledge. But the response to them: Lineage is something that does not admit of revocation after it is established, and an acknowledgment like this is not rejected by rejection. So the acknowledgment remains in effect against himself, preventing his claim, because it is a proof against himself. Like one who acknowledged the freedom of another's slave and the master denies it, then the acknowledgment remains valid in the acknowledger's own favor, so if he ever owns him, he becomes free due to that acknowledgment. The conclusion is that his lineage is not established from the acknowledger, but the boy becomes free upon his claim.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling and an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a custodian's claim of paternity. The basic ruling: if a person in whose custody a boy is, first says the boy is the son of an absent person, then later claims the boy as his own son, the claim is invalid regardless of whether the absent person denies or confirms. This is because the initial acknowledgment creates a right for the absent person, and that acknowledgment cannot be withdrawn. The commentary discusses three scenarios regarding the absent person's response: confirmation, silence, or denial. In the first two, the claim is invalid by consensus. In case of denial, Abu Hanifa holds the claim is still invalid because the acknowledgment remains binding on the acknowledger as a proof against himself. The two Sahibayn argue that the denial by the acknowledged person voids the acknowledgment, so the acknowledger can claim. The Hanafi response uses the analogy of one who acknowledges the freedom of another's slave: the acknowledgment stands even if the owner denies it, so if he later owns the slave, it becomes free. Similarly, here the acknowledgment prevents him from claiming the boy as his own. The practical outcome: the boy does not become his son, but he becomes free as a consequence of the claim.

Key Terms
ذو اليد (dhū al-yad): The person who has physical custody or possession of the boy.
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment: a statement that affirms a right or fact against oneself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, when a custodian acknowledges a boy as the son of an absent person and later claims him as his own, what is the status of the boy?
A) He becomes the custodian's son.
B) He is not the custodian's son but becomes free.
C) He remains the absent person's son.
D) He becomes a free person without lineage.

Key Takeaway
A prior acknowledgment of another's paternity is binding and prevents a later claim of paternity; the claim is invalid but the boy becomes free.

The Boy Claimed by Muslim and Christian: Freedom Prevails
Ruling on a boy claimed as son by a Christian and as slave by a Muslim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a boy is claimed as a slave by a Muslim and as a son by a Christian, and explain the reasoning based on default freedom and religious affiliation.

Core Idea
When a boy is claimed as son by a Christian and as slave by a Muslim, freedom prevails – the boy becomes the Christian’s free son and Muslim by affiliation.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq resolves a conflict between two claimants over a boy: one Muslim claims him as a slave, the other Christian claims him as his son. The Hanafi ruling prioritizes freedom and valid lineage over the Muslim’s claim, illustrating how the school balances principles of default freedom (asl al-hurriyya) and the child's religious affiliation (taba'iyya).

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting claims
The Christian claims the boy as his son; the Muslim claims him as his slave. Both claims are made simultaneously while the boy is in the custody of both.
The timing matters: if the Muslim claims first, the ruling differs.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The boy is declared free (hurr) and becomes the Christian's son. He is also considered Muslim by affiliation to his father.
The ruling is declaratory: it determines status, not an obligation.
3
Trace the reasoning
Freedom is the default legal status. The Christian’s claim of paternity is valid and creates a presumption of free birth. Enslaving the boy would contradict that. Additionally, the boy follows the better religion (Islam) due to the evident proofs of monotheism.
The child’s Islamic affiliation is based on rationality and default disposition (fitra).
4
Note the exceptional case
If the Muslim had claimed the boy as his son (rather than slave), the boy would become his son, because Islam is given precedence. The claim of slavery triggers a different outcome.
The passage emphasizes the importance of the specific wording of the claim.
5
Understand the underlying principle
The Hanafi school resolves such conflicts by prioritizing freedom and, when possible, affirming the Islamic identity of the child. The child’s welfare and default status are key.
This is a manifestation of the maxim al-umur bi-maqasidiha (matters are judged by their intentions) and the principle of asl al-hurriyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان صبي في يد مسلم ونصراني، فقال النصراني: هو ابني، وقال المسلم: هو عبدي، فهو حر، أي إذا كان في يد مسلم ونصراني، فادعى النصراني أنه ابني، وقال المسلم: هو عبدي، وادعيا معا، فالصبي حر، فيكون ابن النصراني، لأنه يكون حراً حالاً، مسلماً تبعاً؛ لظهور دلائل التوحيد لكل عاقل، وفي العكس أي كونه عبداً للمسلم وابناً للنصراني يثبت الإسلام تبعاً، ولا تحصل له الحرية؛ إذ لا قدرة له على اكتسابها، فكان الجمع بينهما أولى، وفيه إشارة إلى أنه لو سبق دعوة المسلم ولم يدعيا معاً، يكون عبداً لله، وكذا في التقييد بقول المسلم: "هو عبدي" إشارة إلى أنه لو قال: "هو ابني" يكون ابنه ترجيحاً له بالإسلام.
And if there is a boy in the hand of a Muslim and a Christian, and the Christian says: he is my son, and the Muslim says: he is my slave, then he is free – meaning: if he is in the hand of a Muslim and a Christian, and the Christian claims he is his son, and the Muslim says he is his slave, and they both claim together, then the boy is free, so he becomes the son of the Christian, because he becomes free immediately, and Muslim by affiliation, due to the evident proofs of monotheism for every rational person. In the opposite case, i.e., him being a slave to the Muslim and a son to the Christian, Islam would be established by affiliation but he would not attain freedom, since he has no ability to earn it. So combining them (freedom and being the Christian's son) is preferable. And in it is an indication that if the Muslim's claim preceded and they did not claim together, he would be a slave of Allah. Likewise, in the restriction by the Muslim's saying 'he is my slave' is an indication that if he said 'he is my son', he would be his son due to the preponderance of Islam.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on a scenario where a boy is found in the custody of a Muslim and a Christian. Both claim the boy: the Christian asserts he is his son, while the Muslim claims him as his slave. The ruling is that the boy is free and becomes the son of the Christian. The commentary explains the reasoning: freedom is granted immediately because it is the default state (asl al-hurriyya) and because the boy cannot be enslaved since the Christian's claim creates a presumption of free birth. Additionally, the boy is considered Muslim by affiliation to his father (the Christian) because the child follows the better religion in matters of status, and Islam is preferred. The commentary notes that if the Muslim had claimed 'he is my son' instead of 'he is my slave,' the boy would have been his son, due to the preponderance of Islam. This ruling shows how the Hanafi school resolves conflicting claims by prioritizing freedom and, where possible, the Islamic identity of the child.

Key Terms
صبي (sabi): A minor boy, a child below puberty.
حر (hurr): Free person, as opposed to slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a boy in the hands of a Muslim and a Christian, if the Christian claims him as son and the Muslim claims him as slave, what is the ruling?
A) The boy becomes the slave of the Muslim.
B) The boy becomes the son of the Christian, free and Muslim.
C) The boy is divided between them.
D) The boy chooses his own status at puberty.

Key Takeaway
When a boy is claimed as a slave by a Muslim and as a son by a Christian, the Hanafi ruling grants the boy freedom and makes him the Christian's son, with Islamic affiliation by default.

Conflicting Claims of Paternity: Joint Custody of Spouses
Ruling on a boy in joint custody with conflicting paternity claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when both husband and wife claim a boy as their child from a previous relationship, and identify the condition that changes the ruling to the boy's testimony.

Core Idea
When spouses equally claim a mute boy as their own, both paternity is established due to balanced evidence and the marital bed (firash).

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a unique scenario where a boy is found in the custody of a married couple, and both claim he is their child from outside the marriage. The classical Hanafi ruling resolves the conflict by accepting both claims, based on the equality of possession and the presumption of firash. However, the boy's own testimony prevails if he can speak.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the claims
Understand that the husband and wife both claim the boy as their own child from another relationship, and the boy is in their joint custody.
The boy is not a newborn; he is old enough to be in custody but possibly mute.
2
Apply the principle of equal evidence
Because both spouses possess the boy equally and their claims are contradictory, neither outweighs the other. There is no preponderance.
Hanafi principle: when evidence is balanced, both claims are accepted if possible.
3
Invoke the firash presumption
The existence of the marital bed (firash) is a strong indicator that the child could be from both spouses.
Firash is a legal presumption of paternity from marriage.
4
Distinguish the boy's capacity
If the boy cannot speak, both paternity is established. If he can speak, his own testimony decides: whichever parent he confirms, that lineage is established.
This is a condition that changes the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان صبي في يد زوجين فالعادة أنه ابنه من غيرها، وزعمت هذه الزوجة أنه ابنها من غيره، فهو ابنهما؛ لأن كل واحد منهما أقر للولد بالنسب، وادعى ما يبطل حق صاحبه، ولا رجحان لأحدهما على الآخر؛ لاستواء أيديهما فيه، وقيام أيديهما عليه، وقيام الفراش بينهما دليل ظاهر على أنه منهما، فيكون ابنهما، ويصح إقرارهما له، ولا يبطل حق صاحبه بمجرد قوله. هذا إذا كان الصبي لا يعبر عن نفسه، وإن كان يعبر فالقول له: أيهما صدق ثبت نسبه منه.
And if there is a boy in the hands of two spouses (husband and wife), customarily he is the son of the husband from another woman, and this wife claims that he is her son from another man, then he is the son of both; because each of them confessed lineage for the child, and claimed what invalidates the right of the other, and there is no preponderance of one over the other due to the equality of their possession of him, and their hand being upon him, and the existence of the marriage bed (firash) between them is an apparent proof that he is from both, so he is the son of both, and their confession for him is valid, and the right of the other is not invalidated by mere saying. This is if the boy cannot express himself; if he can express himself, then the word is his: whichever he confirms, his lineage is established from him.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage addresses a case where a boy is found in the custody of a married couple. The husband claims the boy is his son from another woman, and the wife claims he is her son from another man. The ruling is that the boy is the son of both spouses. The reasoning is that each spouse acknowledges the lineage of the child in a way that contradicts the other's claim, and their hands (possession) are equal. Moreover, the existence of the marital bed (firash) suggests that the child could be from both. Since neither claim is stronger, both are accepted. However, this applies only if the boy is too young to speak or unable to express himself. If the boy can speak, his own testimony decides: whichever parent he confirms becomes his legal parent. This ruling demonstrates the Hanafi reliance on the principle of firash (presumption of paternity from marriage) and the equality of conflicting claims in the absence of preponderance.

Key Terms
زوجين (zawjayn): A married couple (husband and wife).
فراش (firash): The marital bed; legal presumption that a child born to a married woman is the husband's child.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When both spouses claim a boy as their child from another relationship, and the boy cannot speak, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The boy is considered illegitimate.
B) The boy is the son of the husband only.
C) The boy is the son of both spouses.
D) The boy becomes free with no lineage.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when a man and wife equally claim a mute boy as their own child, the boy becomes the son of both, but if the boy can speak, his word decides his lineage.

Child of the Deceived Buyer (al-Maghruur): Free but Value Owed
Ruling on child born to a purchased slave-girl later reclaimed by rightful owner

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the child of a reclaimed slave-girl and evaluate the Companions' dispute over compensation method.

Core Idea
The child of the deceived buyer is free, but the buyer must pay its value to the rightful owner.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq concludes the book of claims with a nuanced case: a man buys a slave-girl, she bears him a child, then the original owner reclaims her. How do we balance the rights of the father (who acted in good faith) and the owner (whose property produced the child)? The Hanafi school follows Ali's view: the child is free, and the father pays the market value. This lesson unpacks the consensus, the dispute between the two Companions, and the Hanafi proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and legal problem
A purchased slave-girl gives birth to a child, then the rightful owner reclaims her with evidence. What is the status of the child? Who owes what to the owner?
2
State the points of consensus
The text claims consensus (ijma') that the child is free and the father (the deceived buyer) is liable for compensation.
No disagreement among the early jurists on these two points.
3
Analyze the dispute between Umar and Ali
They differed on the method: Umar said return a similar child (mithl); Ali said pay the monetary value (qima).
The Hanafi school adopts Ali's view.
4
Follow the Hanafi argument for Ali's view
The Hanafis argue that animals (including human slaves) are not compensable by equivalent (mithl) according to nass. The hadith of Umar is interpreted as the value of a boy or girl.
This is based on the principle that animals are not mithliyyat in legal compensation.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Umar's view, the buyer would have to hand over a child of similar description; under Ali's/Hanafi view, he pays the market value.
The value is assessed on the day of litigation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مشتراة: أي الجارية التي اشتراها المشتري ولدت ولداً وادعى المشتري الولد. فاستحقت الأمة بالبينة غرم الأب قيمة الولد، وهو حر. ختم باب دعوى النسب بمسألة ولد المغرور، والمغرور من وطئ امرأة معتمداً على ملك يمين أو نكاح، فولدت ثم استحقت الوالدة، فولد المغرور حر بالقيمة بالإجماع، فإنه لا خلاف بين الصدر الأول وفقهاء الأمصار أن ولد المغرور حر الأصل، ولا خلاف أنه مضمون على الأب وهو المشتري، إلا أن السلف اختلفوا في كيفية ضمانه، فقال عمر ابن الخطاب: يفك الغلام بالغلام والجارية بالجارية، يعني إن كان الولد غلاماً فعلى الأب غلام مثله، وإن كان جارية فعليه جارية مثلها. وقال علي بن أبي طالب: يضمن قيمتها، وإليه ذهب أصحابنا، فإنه قد ثبت بالنص أن الحيوان لا يكون مضموناً بالمثل، وتأويل الحديث: الغلام بقيمة الغلام والجارية بقيمة الجارية، فمن اشترى جارية فوطئها، فولدت الأمة المشتراة ولداً، فاستحقت الأمة بالبينة غرم الأب أي ضمن المشتري قيمة الولد للمستحق، والولد حر؛ لأنه ولد مغرور؛ إذ المغرور بنى أمره على أمر صحيح في الشرع فاستوجب النظر، والأمة ملك للمستحق، والزائد متفرع عن ملكه، فاستوجب النظر أيضاً، فوجب الجمع بين حقهما بقدر الإمكان بأن يجعل الولد حر الأصل في الأب، ورقيقاً في حق المستحق، فيضمن الأب قيمته يوم الخصومة؛ لأنه يوم المنع، وتجب هذه القيمة على الأب دون الولد، حتى إذا كان الأب ميتاً يؤخذ من تركته، ولا ولاء للمستحق عليه؛ لأنه علق حر الأصل. وكذا إذا ملك الأمة بسبب آخر غير الشراء أي سبب كان، فولدت ثم استحقت. وكذا إذا تزوجها على أنها حرة فولدت ثم استحقت، روي ذلك عن عمر في النكاح، وعن علي في الشراء بمحضر من الصحابة من غير نكير، فكان هذا إجماعاً.
Purchased: i.e., the slave-girl whom the buyer bought gave birth to a child, and the buyer claimed the child. Then the slave-girl was reclaimed by evidence (bayyina), so the father pays the value of the child, and he is free. He concluded the book of claims with the issue of the child of the deceived (al-maghruur). The deceived is one who has intercourse with a woman relying on ownership or marriage, then she gives birth, then the mother is reclaimed. The child of the deceived is free with payment of value by consensus. There is no disagreement among the early generations and the jurists of the regions that the child of the deceived is free origin, and no disagreement that he is guaranteed against the father, who is the buyer. However, the predecessors differed on the manner of guarantee. Umar ibn al-Khattab said: 'The boy is redeemed by a boy, and the girl by a girl,' meaning if the child is a boy, then upon the father a similar boy; if a girl, then upon him a similar girl. Ali ibn Abi Talib said: 'He guarantees its value,' and this is the view of our associates (the Hanafis), because it is established by text that animals are not guaranteed by equivalents, and the interpretation of the hadith is: the boy by the value of a boy, the girl by the value of a girl. So whoever buys a slave-girl and has intercourse with her, and she gives birth, and the slave-girl is reclaimed by evidence, then the father pays – i.e., the buyer guarantees the value of the child to the claimant, and the child is free, because he is the child of a deceived person; since the deceived based his action on something legally sound, he deserves consideration, and the slave-girl is the property of the claimant, and the increase (the child) branches from his property, so he also deserves consideration. So it was necessary to combine their rights as much as possible by making the child free in origin for the father, and a slave in the right of the claimant, so the father guarantees his value on the day of litigation, because that is the day of prevention. This value is obligatory upon the father, not on the child, so that if the father dies, it is taken from his estate. And the claimant does not have wala' (patronage) over him because he was attached to free origin. Similarly, if he owns the slave-girl by another cause other than purchase, any cause, and she gives birth, then is reclaimed. Similarly, if he marries her thinking she is free, and she gives birth, then is reclaimed. This was narrated from Umar regarding marriage, and from Ali regarding purchase, in the presence of the Companions without any objection, so this constitutes consensus (ijma').
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage concludes the chapter on claims (Kitab al-Da'wa) with the case of the child of al-maghruur (the deceived). The scenario: a man buys a slave-girl and has intercourse with her; she gives birth; then a third party proves rightful ownership of the slave-girl (istihqaq). The ruling: the child is free, and the buyer (the 'father') must pay the value of the child to the rightful owner. The text states this is by consensus (ijma'). However, there was a historical dispute between Umar ibn al-Khattab and Ali ibn Abi Talib regarding the method of compensation: Umar advocated for returning a similar child (mithli), while Ali advocated for paying the monetary value (qima). The Hanafi school follows Ali's view, arguing that animals (including human slaves) are not compensable by equivalents in this context, and interpreting Umar's statement as meaning the value. The commentary provides the legal reasoning: the deceived acted on a legally valid basis (ownership or marriage), so his act is respected; the child is an increase from the rightful owner's property, so the owner's right is also respected. To balance both, the child is free in relation to the father but 'enslaved' in terms of value owed to the owner. The value is assessed on the day of litigation (when it is prevented). This ruling also applies if the man owned the slave-girl by any other means, or if he married her thinking she was free. The text claims ijma' (consensus) of the Companions, citing that the reports from Umar and Ali were not objected to in their presence.

Key Terms
مغرور (maghruur): One who is deceived; a person who has intercourse with a woman believing he owns her or is married to her, but she later turns out to belong to another.
استحقاق (istihqaq): The process of claiming property by rightful owner, here the slave-girl being reclaimed by evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when a purchased slave-girl gives birth and is later reclaimed by the rightful owner, what is the status of the child and the liability of the buyer?
A) The child is a slave of the rightful owner, and the buyer is not liable.
B) The child is free, and the buyer must pay its value to the owner.
C) The child is free, and the buyer must return a similar child.
D) The child is free, and the buyer is not liable if he acted in good faith.

Key Takeaway
The child of the deceived buyer is free by consensus, and the buyer must pay its market value to the rightful owner, following Ali ibn Abi Talib's view.

Confession Valid for Unknown Rights
Ruling on validity of confession for unknown rights and obligation to clarify

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a confession (iqrār) is valid even for an unknown right and identify the confessor's obligation to clarify.

Core Idea
A confession is valid even if the right is unknown, but the confessor must clarify and specify something of value.

The Kanz al-Daqa'iq matn lays down a foundational principle in the Hanafi law of confession: the validity of acknowledging an unknown right. This may seem counterintuitive, as confessions typically involve definite obligations. The Hanafi school prioritizes the acknowledgment of liability itself over the need for immediate specificity, while still requiring subsequent clarification. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is iqrār (confession). The ruling from the matn is that it is valid (sahih) even if the right confessed is unknown.
The text says 'ṣaḥḥ iqrāruhu biḥaqq ṣaḥḥ' — his confession regarding a valid right is valid.
2
Note the key condition: the right must be valid
The phrase 'biḥaqq ṣaḥḥ' indicates that the underlying right must be a genuine obligation, not a fabricated one.
This condition prevents frivolous or sham confessions.
3
Understand the obligation to clarify
The matn states 'wa yajibu ʿalā bayānihi' — he must clarify it. The clarification must specify something that has monetary value (mā lahū qīmah).
The kafka in 'ka shay'in wa ḥaqq' gives examples of what is considered unknown.
4
Apply the principle to a scenario
If someone says 'I owe you a right' without details, the confession is binding, but the confessor must later give specifics and pay what is owed.
The confession is not suspended; it takes effect immediately.
5
Recognize cross-madhhab difference
Some schools require the object of confession to be known at the time of utterance; the Hanafi school is more flexible.
The mukhtalaf_fih note in the analytical toolbox indicates this is a point of ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإقرار
أحكام الإقرار
صح إقراره بحق صح، ولو مجهولا كشيء وحق، ويجب على بيانه، ويبين ما له قيمة.
Book of Confession. Rulings of Confession: His confession is valid regarding a right that is valid, even if it is unknown like a thing or a right, and he must clarify it, and he clarifies what has value.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The passage establishes the general principle that a confession (iqrār) is valid even if the right being confessed is unknown (majhūl) in its specific details, such as saying 'I owe you a thing' or 'a right.' However, the confessor (al-muqirr) is obliged to clarify (bayān) what he is confessing to, and he must specify something that has monetary value (mā lahū qīmah). The commentary explains that the ruling applies to cases where the right may have been incurred without precise knowledge, like property damage or injury. The confession is valid because the obligation is established, but the details must be clarified later.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): Confession or acknowledgment of a right against oneself.
مقر (muqirr): The person making the confession.
مقر له (muqarr lahu): The person in whose favor the confession is made.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must the confessor do if he confesses to an unknown right?
A) Nothing
B) Clarify what has value
C) Pay immediately
D) Recant

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a confession is valid even for an unknown right, but the confessor must later clarify and specify something of value.

Confessor's Statement and Oath
Ruling on the confessor's statement and oath when claimant claims more

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on accepting the confessor's statement with oath and identify the exception for property.

Core Idea
The confessor's word with oath settles his admission; the claimant's excess is rejected.

In disputes over the amount of a confessed right, the confessor's own statement is given weight, but only with an oath. This lesson examines the Hanafi rule that protects the confessor from being forced to pay more than he admits, while also noting a limitation in property cases where his word is not accepted if he claims an amount below a certain threshold.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
The confessor (muqirr) is the one who made the admission; the claimant (muqarr lahu) is the one claiming more.
The confessor is the denier of the excess amount.
2
Recognize the dispute
The dispute is over the quantity: the claimant claims more than what the confessor admits.
This creates a conflict of statements.
3
Apply the general rule
The confessor's statement is taken with his oath, so he only owes what he admits.
The rule is based on the principle that the denier's word is accepted with oath.
4
Note the exception for property
In matters of property (mal), if the confessor claims an amount less than a certain minimum, his statement is not believed.
The minimum is defined elsewhere; effectively, he is not believed for trivial amounts.
5
Understand the rationale
The confessor is considered a denier (munkir) of the excess, so his statement is given weight, but the exception prevents abuse in undervaluing property.
The oath is required to affirm his denial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول متعلق بالمقر أي شيئاً للمقر مع يمينه إن ادعى المقر له أكثر منه، وفي مال لم يصدق في أقل.
And the statement is related to the confessor: i.e., it is something for the confessor with his oath if the claimant claims more than that, and in property he is not believed in less.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This ruling establishes that in a dispute over the amount of a confessed right, the confessor's (al-muqirr) word is taken with his oath (yamin) if the claimant (al-muqarr lahu) claims more than what the confessor acknowledges. However, in matters of property (mal), the confessor is not believed if he claims the amount is less than a certain minimum (the definition of 'mal' is elaborated later). The commentary explains that the confessor is the denier (munkir) regarding the excess, so his statement is accepted with oath.

Key Terms
قول (qawl): The statement or claim of the confessor in court.
مقر (muqirr): The confessor, the person who makes an admission (iqrar).
مقر له (muqarr lahu): The claimant, the person in whose favor the confession is made.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When he claims more than the claimant.
B) When he claims less than a certain minimum.
C) When he refuses to take an oath.
D) When the claimant takes an oath.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule: the confessor's statement with oath is accepted for the amount he admits; but in property, he is not believed if he claims less than the minimum.

Defining 'Great Wealth' in Acknowledgment: The Hanafi Ruling
Explanation of the ruling for acknowledging an unspecified great wealth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on acknowledging an unspecified 'great wealth' and apply the nisab-based interpretation.

Core Idea
An acknowledgment of 'great wealth' obligates at least the nisab of zakat.

When someone acknowledges a debt as 'great wealth' without specifying an amount, how do we determine the minimum due? This passage explores the Hanafi approach: 'great' is defined by the Shari'ah standard of nisab, but there is a nuanced intra-Hanafi debate about the role of the acknowledger's own financial status. Understanding this ruling sharpens your ability to interpret ambiguous legal language with both textual and customary evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The text states that an acknowledgment of 'great wealth' obligates a nisab. Why? Because the description 'great' must be given effect, and nisab is considered great in both Shari'ah and custom.
The rule applies when no specific amount is mentioned.
2
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) require the full zakat nisab (200 dirhams for silver) for everyone. Another narration from Abu Hanifa accepts 10 dirhams. Al-Sarakhsi reconciles by considering the acknowledger's wealth: the poor are bound by 10, the rich by 200.
This shows how usul principles (combining texts, considering custom and status) resolve apparent contradictions.
3
Apply the ruling to other zakatable assets
The same principle extends to gold (20 mithqals), camels (25), and all zakatable categories. The nisab for each type is the minimum due.
This ensures consistency across different wealth types.
4
Compare with other schools
Imam al-Shafi'i and Ahmad ibn Hanbal hold that any amount qualifies as 'great'. The Hanafi position relies on coupling the legal definition (nisab in zakat) with customary perception.
The khilaf is rooted in how 'greatness' is determined: by Shari'ah definitions vs. linguistic generality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومال عظيم أي لو قال: لفلان علي مال عظيم يجب عليه نصاب؛ لأن قوله إقرار بمال موصوف بالعظم، فيعتبر هذا الوصف، والنصاب عظيم في الشرع حتى اعتبر صاحبه غنياً، وكذا عرفاً حتى يعد من الأغنياء عادة، فإن أقر أنه الفضة لم يصدق في أقل من مائتي درهم سواء كان غنياً أو فقيراً، وهذا قولهما، وهو رواية عن من أبي حنيفة، قيل: وهو الصحيح؛ لأنه لم يذكر عدداً يوجب مراعاة اللفظ فيه فأوجبنا العظيم من حيث المعنى، وهو المال الذي تجب فيه الزكاة وعنه أنه يصدق في عشرة دراهم؛ لأنه نصاب السرقة والمهر، وهو عظيم حيث تقطع به اليد المحترمة ويباح به البضع المحترم، وذكر شمس الأئمة السرخسي: الصحيح عنده أن المقر لو كان فقيراً يصدق في عشرة، وإن كان غنياً فعليه مائتا درهم؛ لأن القليل عند الفقير عظيم، وأضعاف ذلك عند الغني ليس بعظيم، وهو الأصح، ومن الذهب لا يصدق في أقل من عشرين مثقالاً، وفي كل جنس من أجناس مال الزكاة لا يصدق في أقل من قدر النصاب، حتى لا يصدق في الإبل في أقل من خمس وعشرين؛ لأنه أقل نصاب تجب فيه الزكاة من جنسه، وعند الشافعي وأحمد يصدق في قليل وكثير.
And 'great wealth': meaning if he said: 'I owe so-and-so great wealth', then a nisab is obligatory upon him, because his statement is an acknowledgment of wealth described as great, so this description is considered. And the nisab is great in the Shari'ah to the extent that its owner is considered wealthy, and likewise in custom ('urf) to the extent that he is counted among the wealthy normally. If he acknowledges that it is silver, he is not believed in less than two hundred dirhams, whether he is rich or poor. This is the view of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and it is a narration from Abu Hanifah. It is said that this is the correct view, because he did not mention a number that would require considering the wording, so we obligated the great in terms of meaning, which is wealth upon which zakat is obligatory. And from him (Abu Hanifah) there is a narration that he is believed for ten dirhams, because it is the nisab of theft and dowry, and it is great because the respected hand is cut off for it and the respected private part is made permissible by it. And Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi mentioned: the correct view according to him is that if the acknowledger is poor, he is believed for ten, and if he is rich, then two hundred dirhams are due from him, because little is great for the poor, and multiples of that are not great for the rich. And this is the most correct. And for gold, he is not believed in less than twenty mithqals. And for every genus of zakatable wealth, he is not believed in less than the amount of the nisab, such that he is not believed for camels in less than twenty-five, because that is the least nisab upon which zakat is due from its genus. And according to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad, he is believed in little and much.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school on interpreting an acknowledgment of 'great wealth' (mal 'azim) when no specific amount is mentioned. The author explains that 'great' is defined by the nisab of zakat, which is the threshold for wealth in Islamic law and custom. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) require the full zakat nisab (200 dirhams of silver or equivalent), while a narration from Abu Hanifa accepts 10 dirhams (the nisab for theft and mahr). Al-Sarakhsi reconciles these by considering the financial status of the acknowledger: the poor are believed for 10 dirhams, the rich for 200, which is deemed the most correct (al-asahh). The ruling extends to all zakatable wealth, requiring the nisab of each type. The passage contrasts the Hanafi view with the Shafi'i and Hanbali position, which allows any amount. The commentary follows the text's order, first presenting the general rule, then the differences, then the preferred view, and finally the cross-school comparison.

Key Terms
مال عظيم (mal 'azim): Wealth described as 'great' in an acknowledgment; interpreted here as at least the nisab of zakat.
نصاب (nisab): The minimum amount of wealth upon which zakat is obligatory (e.g., 200 dirhams of silver or 20 mithqals of gold).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone acknowledges a debt of 'great wealth' and does not specify the amount, what is the minimum he must pay?
A) 10 dirhams
B) 200 dirhams
C) Whatever the acknowledger states
D) It depends on the nisab of theft

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an unspecified acknowledgment of 'great wealth' is interpreted by default as the nisab of zakat, with a nuanced exception for the poor.

Acknowledging 'Great Wealths': The Hanafi Ruling on Plural Obligations
Explanation of the ruling for acknowledging multiple great wealths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi linguistic principle that the minimum plural is three to determine the required payment when someone acknowledges owing multiple 'great wealths'.

Core Idea
Acknowledging 'great wealths' binds you to three full nisabs.

In the Hanafi school, the interpretation of words in legal acknowledgments (iqrar) follows their linguistic meanings. When someone uses the plural 'amwal' (wealths), the school's grammatical rule that the minimum plural is three triggers an obligation of three separate nisabs. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its basis, including how to handle non-zakatable items and the principle of certainty (al-yaqin).

Guided Path
1
Identify the acknowledgment
Recognize that the phrase 'great wealths' (amwal 'azimah) is a plural noun. The Hanafi school treats this as indicating at least three entities.
The plural form jama' is key.
2
Apply the linguistic rule
Since the minimum of a plural is three, the acknowledgment obligates three full nisabs from the same genus as specified.
No room for interpretation of less than three.
3
Handle specification of non-zakatable wealth
If the acknowledger clarifies that the wealth is not zakatable (e.g., personal property), its total value must equal the value of three nisabs.
Value is used if the items are not of a type subject to zakat.
4
Apply the certainty principle for nisab value
To ensure certainty, the lowest nisab (in value) is used when calculating the required amount.
The lesser amount is certain, avoiding any possibility of underpayment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أموال عظام أي لو قال: 'لفلان علي أموال عظام' لزمه ثلاثة نصب من جنس ما سماه. (عيني) ثلاثة نصب: من أي مال فسره؛ لأن أقل الجمع ثلاثة، فلا يصدق في أقل منه، وإن بينه بغير مال الزكاة يعتبر أن تبلغ قيمته قدر ثلاثة نصب، وتعتبر الأدنى في ذلك؛ للتيقن به أي أدنى النصب من حيث القيمة؛ لأن الأقل هو المتيقن.

(Ayni) Three nisabs: from whatever wealth he interprets it as, because the minimum of a plural is three, so he is not believed in less than that. And if he specifies it as non-zakatable wealth, its value must reach the amount of three nisabs, and the lowest (nisab) is considered in that, for certainty, meaning the lowest nisab in terms of value, because the lesser is what is certain.

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when someone acknowledges 'great wealths' in the plural. The Hanafi school holds that the plural implies at least three, so three full nisabs are required. If the acknowledger specifies a non-zakatable item, the value must equal three nisabs, and the nisab of the least valuable type is used to ensure certainty (since the lesser is certain). The text cites al-Ayni as the source. The ruling is based on the linguistic principle that the minimum of a plural (jama') is three, and iqrar is interpreted according to apparent meaning unless proven otherwise.

Key Terms
أموال عظام (amwal 'azimah): Plural of 'great wealth'; here interpreted as at least three separate entities each meeting the nisab.
الجمع (al-jam'): The grammatical plural in Arabic; here the minimum number for a plural is three.
