The Ambiguous 'Fi': One Garment or Eleven?
Dispute on the interpretation of the preposition 'fi' in an admission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) interprets the admission 'a garment in ten garments' as one garment, and evaluate Muhammad's counter-argument using the principles of custom and bara'at al-dhimmah.

Core Idea
When 'fi' can mean 'in' or 'among', the default is bara'at al-dhimmah — only one garment is owed.

The Arabic preposition 'fi' (في) is versatile: it can indicate containment ('in') or companionship ('among'). This ambiguity leads to a fascinating legal dispute within the Hanafi school. When someone admits 'I owe you a garment in ten garments,' does he owe one garment (because 'fi' means 'among') or eleven garments (because 'fi' means 'inside' and the ten are containers)? This lesson unpacks the reasoning of the great Hanafi imams, showing how custom and legal principles resolve the ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
State the Conflicting Positions
Identify the two views: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (and al-Shafi'i) say one garment; Muhammad says eleven garments.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, but note that al-Shafi'i agrees with the majority of the Hanafi school.
2
Analyze Linguistic Possibility
Muhammad argues that 'fi' can mean 'inside' because a valuable garment may be wrapped in ten garments, making the ten a container. He analogizes to 'wheat in a sack.'
He focuses on the literal possibility of containment.
3
Apply Customary Impossibility
The two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) counter that it is not customary for ten garments to serve as a container for one. What is impossible customarily is treated as impossible legally.
They rely on 'urf (custom) to restrict the literal meaning.
4
Use the Quranic Precedent
They cite Quran 89:29: 'Enter among My servants' (فَادْخُلِي فِي عِبَادِي) where 'fi' means 'among', not 'inside'. This shows the word is ambiguous.
The dalil is from the Quran, but its dalala (indication) is zanni due to the multiple meanings of 'fi'.
5
Apply Bara'at al-Dhimmah
Since ambiguity remains, the default principle is that the admitter's liability is free from extra obligation. Thus only one garment is owed.
This is a core Hanafi usul principle: in doubt, do not impose additional liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبثوب في عشرة له ثوب أي لو أقر رجل لآخر بثوب في عشرة أثواب ( عيني) له ثوب أي واحد عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف والشافعي، وقال محمد له أحد عشر ثوباً؛ لأن النفيس من الثياب قد يلف في عشرة، فأمكن جعله ظرفاً كقوله: علي حنطة في الجوالق، وللشيخين أن العشرة لا تكون ظرفاً لثوب واحد عادةً، والممتنع عادة كالممتنع حقيقة، ولأن الجميع ليس بوعاء للواحد، بل كان كل واحد منها موعى بما حواه، والوعاء الذي ليس بموعي هو ما كان ظاهراً، فإذا تحقق عدم كون العشرة وعاء للثوب الواحد، فيتعذر حمله على الظرف، فيتعين أول كلامه مجملاً، وحرف "في" يستعمل في البين والوسط أيضاً، قال الله تعالى: ﴿فَادْخُلِي فِي عِبَادِي (الفجر: ٢٩) أي بين عبادي، فوقع الشك، والأصل براءة الذمم ، فسقط عند عشرة أثواب . (عيني، عناية، جوهري )
And for a garment in ten garments, he has one garment: i.e., if a man admitted to another a garment in ten garments (Ayni), he has one garment, i.e., one, according to Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and al-Shafi'i. Muhammad said: he has eleven garments, because a valuable garment may be wrapped in ten, so it is possible to make it a container, like his saying: 'I have wheat in the sack.' The two shaykhs argue that ten cannot be a container for one garment customarily, and what is impossible customarily is like what is impossible in reality. Also, the whole is not a vessel for the one; rather each one of them is a vessel for what it contains, and a vessel that is not contained is what is apparent. So when it is established that ten are not a vessel for the one garment, it becomes impossible to interpret it as a container; thus the first part of his speech is mujmal (ambiguous). And the letter 'fi' is also used for 'among' and 'amidst', as Allah said: 'Enter among My servants' (Quran 89:29), i.e., among My servants. So doubt arises, and the default is immunity of liability (bara'at al-dhimmah), so the extra is dropped; thus one garment is owed, not ten. (Ayni, Inayah, Jawhari)
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute (and also a cross-madhhab alignment) concerning the admission 'I owe you a garment in ten garments' (ثوب في عشرة أثواب). Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that only one garment is owed because it is not customary for ten garments to serve as a container for a single garment; the word 'fi' here is interpreted as 'among' (like 'among My servants'), creating ambiguity, and the default principle (bara'at al-dhimmah) dictates that no extra garments are owed. Muhammad al-Shaybani, however, holds that eleven garments are owed because a valuable garment might be wrapped inside ten others, making the ten a viable container (like wheat in a sack). The two shaykhs argue by 'customary impossibility' (الممتنع عادة كالممتنع حقيقة) – what is impossible customarily is treated as impossible legally. The passage also notes that al-Shafi'i agrees with Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. The relied-upon position is that of the majority (the two shaykhs), so only one garment is owed.

Key Terms
براءة الذمة (bara'at al-dhimmah): The legal presumption that a person's liability is free from any debt or obligation until proven otherwise.
ممتنع عادة (mumtani' 'adatan): Impossible customarily; something that is not done in customary practice.
في (fi): A preposition meaning 'in' or 'among'; its ambiguity in this context leads to different interpretations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), if someone admits 'I owe you a garment in ten garments' (لك علي ثوب في عشرة أثواب), how many garments does he owe?
A) One garment
B) Ten garments
C) Eleven garments
D) One garment plus one-tenth of each of the ten

Key Takeaway
When an admission contains an ambiguous term like 'fi', Hanafi law applies custom to resolve ambiguity and defaults to bara'at al-dhimmah, protecting the admitter from extra liability.

Ruling on Admitting to Five in Five: Numerical Interpretation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether 'five in five' means five, ten, or twenty-five.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the admission 'five in five' and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement among Zufar, Hasan ibn Ziyad, and the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, an ambiguous admission is interpreted minimally; 'five in five' means five, not twenty-five.

This passage examines a specific case of iqrar (admission) where a person says 'I owe you five in five' (خمسة في خمسة). The Hanafi school (Zahir al-Riwayah) rules that only five dirhams are owed because the additional phrase is considered superfluous and the default presumption of innocence (bara'at al-dhimmah) applies. However, other Hanafi jurists such as Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad proposed different interpretations—ten and twenty-five dirhams respectively—leading to an intra-madhhab debate.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school (represented by 'عندنا') holds that five dirhams are owed. The admission is taken literally as five, and the second 'five' is deemed meaningless.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi disagreements
Zufar (a prominent Hanafi student) said ten dirhams are owed, interpreting 'in' as addition (5+5). Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad said twenty-five, interpreting it as multiplication (5×5). Ahmad ibn Hanbal also held the latter view.
3
Follow the reasoning of the opposing views
Zufar's reasoning: the phrase 'five in five' implies two fives, so ten. Hasan's reasoning: 'in' in common parlance can denote multiplication, so five times five equals twenty-five.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school rejects both interpretations because in legal admissions, the default is to minimize liability; the additional phrase 'in five' is not a valid condition or container, so it is ignored. The confession is valid only for the explicitly stated five.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
The practical difference: depending on the interpretation, the confessor owes five, ten, or twenty-five. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is five dirhams, as stated in the text 'يجب خمسة عندنا'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبخمسة في خمسة، وعنى به الضرب خمسة أي لو أقر رجل بخمسة في خمسة (عيني) وعنى به أي والحال أن المقر قصد بالمقر به الضرب. (عيني) خمسة أي يجب خمسة عندنا، وقال زفر عليه عشرة وقال الحسن ابن زياد عليه خمسة وعشرون بعرف الحساب، وفي "التقريب": مذهب زفر مثل قول الحسن وكذا قال في "المجمع": وإن أراد الحساب الزمناه بخمسة لا خمسة وعشرين، فهذا يدل على أن اللازم عند زفر خمسة وعشرون كما هو مذهب الحسن، وهو قول أحمد،
And for five in five, meaning he intended the multiplication (the number) five. That is, if a man admitted to five in five (Ayni), and he meant by that that the confessor intended by the confessed the multiplication (Ayni). Five, i.e., five is owed according to us (Hanafis). Zufar said: ten is owed. Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad said: twenty-five is owed according to the way of calculation. In al-Taqrib: Zufar's madhhab is like the statement of al-Hasan. Similarly, it is said in al-Majma': 'If he intended calculation, we would oblige him with five, not twenty-five.' This indicates that what is owed according to Zufar is twenty-five, as is the madhhab of al-Hasan, and it is the statement of Ahmad.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This final passage deals with the admission 'five in five' (خمسة في خمسة) where the confessor intends the mathematical operation (multiplication – ضرب). According to the Hanafi school (represented by the text's 'at our place' – عندنا), only five dirhams are owed because the second 'five' is considered superfluous or not a valid container. Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl said ten is owed (five plus five). Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad (a Hanafi narrator of Nawadir) said twenty-five (five times five) is owed based on calculation. The text notes that Zufar's view is similar to al-Hasan's (i.e., twenty-five), and Ahmad ibn Hanbal also holds that twenty-five is owed. However, the Hanafi school (the text says 'at our place') maintains that only five is owed, because the admission is interpreted literally as five dirhams, and the additional 'in five' is meaningless or creates doubt, so the default (bara'at al-dhimmah) applies, limiting it to five. This reflects the broader Hanafi principle of construing admissions narrowly against the confessor when ambiguity arises.

Key Terms
ضرب (darb): Multiplication; in this context, the confessor intends the number five as a result of multiplying five by five? But the text says he intended the 'darb' five, meaning he meant the number five from multiplication? Actually, the text is unclear: 'وعنى به الضرب خمسة' – it means he intended the multiplication to yield five. The Hanafis interpret it as just five.
حساب (hisab): Arithmetic calculation; al-Hasan ibn Ziyad interprets by calculation as five times five = twenty-five.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Five dirhams
B) Ten dirhams
C) Twenty-five dirhams

Acknowledgment of a Hajlah: Wood and Covering Included
Ruling on acknowledgment of a hajlah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on acknowledgment of a hajlah and explain why the wood and covering are included based on customary usage.

Core Idea
Acknowledging a hajlah conveys its entire customary assembly — wood and covering.

This lesson examines a specific ruling on the acknowledgment of composite objects. When someone acknowledges a hajlah (a bridal chamber), the recipient receives both its wooden structure and its cloth covering. The reasoning is based on linguistic custom: the term 'hajlah' encompasses the customary components. We will walk through the ruling, the underlying principle, and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is the acknowledgment (iqrar) of a hajlah. The ruling (hukm) is that the wood and covering belong to the recipient.
2
Understand the proof (dalil)
The proof is rational (dalil aqli): the name 'hajlah' is customarily understood to include both wood and covering. The commentator states: 'because the name applies to this whole.'
This is based on 'urf (custom) and linguistic usage.
3
Recognize the underlying principle
In Hanafi fiqh, when a term is used for a composite object, it includes all parts that are customarily part of it, unless specified otherwise.
This principle applies to other composite objects like houses, ships, etc.
4
Check for ikhtilaf
There is no disagreement (ikhtilaf) reported from the Hanafi school. The ruling is consistent across the madhhab.
Thus it is considered muttafaq alayh within the school.
5
Apply to practice
If a person says, 'I acknowledge this hajlah to you,' the recipient takes ownership of the wood and the cloth coverings without needing separate mention.
This avoids future disputes about what was intended.

Interpreting 'Five in Five': Multiplicity vs. Accompaniment
Ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on ambiguous acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the acknowledgment phrase 'five in five' when multiplication is intended versus accompaniment, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement among Abu Hanifa, Zufar, and Hasan ibn Ziyad.

Core Idea
In ambiguous acknowledgments, debtor's intent determines liability, and the minimum is taken to protect the debtor.

When someone acknowledges a debt with ambiguous phrasing, how do we determine the amount owed? The Hanafi school gives weight to the acknowledger's intention. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq examines the phrase 'five in five' and explores three possible interpretations: five, ten, or twenty-five. The preferred view is that if multiplication is intended, the debtor owes five; if accompaniment is intended, he owes ten.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous phrase
The phrase 'five in five' (خمسة في خمسة) is linguistically ambiguous – it could mean multiplication (5×5), addition (5+5), or simple repetition.
This ambiguity triggers the need for interpretation.
2
State the Hanafi position
According to Abu Hanifa (the position of the school 'عندنا'), if the acknowledger intended multiplication, he owes only five (the first number). If he intended 'with' (accompaniment), he owes ten.
The rationale: the phrase plausibly carries both meanings; the debtor is believed about his intent because he is in a position of burden.
3
Identify the opposing intra-Hanafi views
Zufar held that 'in' implies addition, so the debtor owes ten (5+5). Hasan ibn Ziyad held that 'in' implies multiplication, so the debtor owes twenty-five (5×5).
These are legitimate differences within the Hanafi school, though not the relied-upon view.
4
Understand the tarjih (preferred view)
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa: five if multiplication, ten if 'with'. If his intent is unknown, the minimum (five) is taken as a precaution, because the debtor's liability is established by a doubtful cause.
This protects the debtor from excessive claims based on ambiguous wording.
5
Apply to a scenario
If a person says 'I owe you five in five' and later claims he meant multiplication, he owes five. If he says he meant 'with', he owes ten. If he is silent, the default is multiplication and he owes five.
The qadi would ask the debtor to clarify his intent; if he persists on multiplication, five is imposed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

But the phrasing is confusing: it says 'five' is obligatory, implying maybe he owes five (the result of multiplication? Actually 5x5=25, so why five? The commentators explain: if he says 'five in five' meaning multiplication, the word 'in' is interpreted as multiplication in common arithmetic, but the Hanafi school says he only owes five because that is the literal meaning of the phrase? Acknowledgment of a Range: Inclusion of Endpoints in Hanafi Law
Intra-Hanafi dispute on range acknowledgment endpoints

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether a range acknowledgment includes the endpoints and apply the reasoning to determine the owed amount.

Core Idea
When you say 'from one to ten', the first is included but the last is excluded—giving nine.

This passage examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: if someone acknowledges a debt 'from one dirham to ten,' how many dirhams does he owe? The answer depends on whether the endpoints are included. Abu Hanifa, his two main disciples, and Zufar all differ. The reasoning involves the principle of ghāyah (limit) and a necessary exception for the starting point.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
A person acknowledges a debt using a range phrase: 'from one dirham to ten' or 'between one dirham and ten'. What is the legal issue?
2
State the three Hanafi positions
List Abu Hanifa (nine), Abu Yusuf & Muhammad (ten), Zufar (eight).
Remember: each imam has a distinct ruling on the number of dirhams owed.
3
Understand the general principle (ghāyah)
The limit (ghāyah) is not included in the limited (mughayyā), as in the wall analogy where neither wall is included by consensus.
This is the default rule from which exceptions may arise.
4
Apply the exception for numbers
The first limit (one) must be included out of necessity because the second dirham cannot exist without the first. The last limit (ten) is not necessary, so it is excluded by qiyas.
Necessity (darurah) overrides the general principle only for the starting point.
5
Derive the mufta bihi ruling
Abu Hanifa's view (nine) is the operative position, as upheld by later authorities.
The other positions are respected but not followed for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
له تسعة له علي من درهم إلى عشرة، أو ما بين درهم إلى عشرة، | له تسعة أي لو قال لفلان" عليّ من درهم إلى عشرة، أو قال: "له علي ما بين درهم إلى عشرة"، يكون للمقر له تسعة دراهم عند أبي حنيفة وقالا يلزمه عشرة، وهو قول الثلاثة، وفي قول لهم: عليه تسعة، وعند زفر ثمانية، والأصل في ذلك أن عنده يدخل الابتداء، ولا يدخل الانتهاء، وعندهما: يدخلان جميعاً، زفر: لا يدخلان جميعاً، وهو القياس، كما لو قال: لفلان من هذا الحائط إلى هذا الحائط، أو ما بين هذين الحائطين، فإن الحائطين لا يدخلان في الإقرار بالإجماع. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٢٠/٢] وله أن الغاية لا تدخل تحت المغيا؛ لأن الحد غير المحدود، فهذا هو الأصل كما قال زفر، لكن هنا لابد من إدخال الأولى؛ لأن الدرهم الثاني والثالث لا يتحقق بدون الأول؛ إذ لا يعقل الثاني بدون الأول، فدخلت الغاية الأولى ضرورة، ولا ضرورة في إدخال الثاني، فأخذنا فيها بالقياس فلا يدخل، ولأن العدد يقتضي ابتداء، فإذا الأول من أن يكون ابتداء صار الثاني هو الابتداء، فيخرج هو أيضاً من أن يكون ابتداء كالأول، وكذا خرج له الثالث والرابع إلى آخره، فيؤدي إلى إخراج الكل من أن يكون واجباً فكان باطلاً، فتعين ما قلنا. (فتح)
For the recipient is nine: he has due from me from one dirham to ten, or what is between one dirham to ten. | For the recipient is nine: i.e., if he said to someone, 'I owe from one dirham to ten,' or he said, 'I owe what is between one dirham to ten,' then for the recipient are nine dirhams according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: ten are obligatory, and it is the saying of the three (i.e., the three caliphs? Actually 'al-thalāthah' refers to the three imams besides Hanifa? Unclear). And in one saying of theirs: on him is nine. And according to Zufar: eight. And the basis of this is that according to him (Abu Hanifa), the beginning is included but the end is not. According to them (the two): both are included. According to Zufar: neither is included, and that is the qiyas (analogy), as if he said: 'For so-and-so from this wall to this wall, or what is between these two walls,' then the two walls are not included in the acknowledgment by consensus. [Ramz al-Haqaiq 2/220] And for him (Abu Hanifa): the limit (ghāyah) is not included under the limited (mughayyā), because the boundary is not the bounded. This is the original principle as Zufar said, but here the first (starting point) must be included because the second and third dirhams cannot be realized without the first, since the second is inconceivable without the first. So the first limit is included out of necessity, and there is no necessity for including the second. Therefore we apply qiyas to it and it is not included. And because number requires a beginning, so if the first is considered as the beginning, the second becomes the beginning, and it would also be excluded from being the beginning like the first, and likewise the third, fourth, etc., leading to the exclusion of all from being obligatory, which would be void. So what we said is determined. (Fath)
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage discusses the acknowledgment of a range: 'from one dirham to ten' or 'between one dirham and ten'. The Hanafi imams differ on whether the endpoints are included. Abu Hanifa holds that the starting point (one dirham) is included out of necessity, but the ending point (ten) is not, so the recipient gets nine dirhams (2-9? Actually 1-9? Wait: 'from one to ten' if one is included and ten excluded, that gives numbers 1 through 9, which is nine dirhams). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say both endpoints are included, giving ten dirhams (1-10). Zufar says neither is included, giving eight dirhams (2-9). The commentator explains that the general principle is that the limit (ghāyah) is not included under the limited (mughayyā), as in the case of walls. However, in the case of numbers, the first limit must be included because without the first, the second cannot exist. The other limits (2-9) are included by necessity as well? Actually the reasoning: only the first is necessary; the last is not. So the obligatory amount is from the first to the last minus one, i.e., nine. The qiyas with walls is given, but note that in the wall case, neither wall is included by consensus. The commentator refines the qiyas for numbers.

Key Terms
غاية (ghāyah): Limit, endpoint; in this context, the number 'ten' or the second limit
مغيا (mughayyā): That which is limited or bounded by the ghāyah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Eight
B) Nine
C) Ten
D) The acknowledgment is invalid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a range acknowledgment includes the starting point but excludes the ending point, resulting in the owed number being one less than the declared upper limit if the lower limit is one.

Acknowledgment of a House Between Two Walls: Limit Exclusion
Ruling on acknowledgment of a house between two walls

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for acknowledgment of a property portion defined by two walls and explain the principle that the limit (ghāyah) is excluded from the limited (mughayyā).

Core Idea
'Between two walls' gives only the interior space – the limit is excluded from the limited.

This passage clarifies a specific case of property acknowledgment. When someone says 'for so-and-so from my house between this wall and that wall,' only the enclosed space is transferred. The walls remain with the original owner because the legal principle excludes the limit from the limited, a rule confirmed by later commentators like al-‘Aynī.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wording
Examine the phrase 'between this wall and that wall' – this defines the portion by its boundaries.
The acknowledgment explicitly mentions the two walls as limits.
2
Determine what is included
Only the interior space between the walls is given. The recipient acquires the area enclosed, not the walls themselves.
3
Recognize what is excluded
The walls are excluded because they are the limits (ghāyah) of the described portion. In physical objects, the limit is not part of the limited.
This parallels the earlier rule on numbers: when counting, the limit is not included.
4
Apply the principle
State the ruling: the acknowledgment is valid, and only the space between the two walls is transferred. The walls remain the property of the original owner.
No necessity to include any part of the walls arises here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

'Only': meaning without the two walls, because the limit is not included under the limited in physical things, nor is the starting point. (Ayni)

Commentary
This short passage provides a clear ruling on the acknowledgment of a portion of a house defined by two walls. When someone says 'for so-and-so from my house between this wall and that wall,' the recipient receives only the space between the walls, not the walls themselves. The commentator explains that this is because the limit (ghāyah) is not included in the limited (mughayyā) in tangible things; neither the starting wall nor the ending wall is included. This is a straightforward application of the same principle discussed in the previous lesson regarding numbers, but here there is no necessity to include any part, so both walls are excluded.

Validity of Acknowledgment of Pregnancy
Ruling that acknowledgment of pregnancy is valid with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for valid acknowledgment of pregnancy and explain the interpretative mechanism (bequest) used by Hanafi jurists.

Core Idea
Acknowledgment of pregnancy is valid only as a reference to a prior bequest, not as direct transfer.

This lesson addresses a specific muamalat ruling: the validity of acknowledging a pregnancy (of a slave-girl or animal) as belonging to someone else. Hanafi jurists hold that such an acknowledgment cannot be taken literally because the fetus is not a separate owned entity. Instead, they interpret it as an heir's acknowledgment of a bequest made by the deceased owner. We will examine the conditions and the reasoning behind this interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its apparent problem
What is the acknowledgment? Why can't it be literal? Recognize that the fetus is not independently owned, so a direct transfer is impossible.
The acknowledgment must have a plausible legal basis.
2
Understand the Hanafi solution
How is the acknowledgment reinterpreted? It is treated as a reference to a prior bequest from a third party, whose heir now acknowledges the bequest.
This is the only way to validate the acknowledgment by consensus.
3
Distinguish between slave-girl and animal cases
What conditions differ? For a slave-girl, probability of existence suffices; for animals, certainty is required.
Why? Human pregnancy may extend up to two years (iddah), but animal gestation is fixed.
4
Apply gestation period rules
How do we verify existence at acknowledgment? Use maximum possible delay: 6 months if married, 2 years if in iddah for humans; for animals, expert-determined minimum periods (4 months sheep, 6 months others).
If birth occurs beyond these limits, acknowledgment is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح الإقرار بالحمل بأن قال: حمل جاريتي هذه، أو حمل شاتي هذه لفلان. (عيني)] وإن لم يبين السبب بالإجماع؛ لأن لتصحيحه جهة واحدة، وهي الوصية من جهة غيره بأن يحمل هذا الإقرار على أن رجلاً أوصى بالحمل لرجل، ومات الوصي، والآن يقر وارثه بأنه للموصى له، ثم إن كان يقر به حمل جارية يستحقه المقر له إذا علم وجوده عند الإقرار أو احتمل ذلك بأن تضعه لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الإقرار إن كانت ذات زوج أو لأقل من سنتين إذا كانت معتدة، وإن كان حمل شاة يشترط التيقن بوجوده وقت الإقرار، فيقدر بأدنى مدة يتصور ذلك عند أهل الخبرة على ما جرت به عادتهم، وأقل مدة حمل الشاة أربعة أشهر وأقلها لبقية الدواب ستة أشهر. (عيني، فتح)
And the acknowledgment of pregnancy is valid, such as when he says: 'the pregnancy of this slave-girl of mine' or 'the pregnancy of this sheep of mine is for so-and-so.' (Ayni). Even if he does not state the reason, by consensus, because there is only one way for it to be valid: a bequest from someone else, meaning that this acknowledgment is interpreted as a man bequeathed the pregnancy to a man, and the executor died, and now his heir acknowledges that it is for the legatee. Then if it is the pregnancy of a slave-girl, the recipient is entitled to it if its existence at the time of acknowledgment is known or probable, such that she gives birth less than six months from the time of acknowledgment if she has a husband, or less than two years if she is in her waiting period (iddah). If it is the pregnancy of a sheep, then certainty of its existence at the time of acknowledgment is required, and it is estimated by the minimum period in which that is possible according to experts, according to their custom. The minimum period of pregnancy for a sheep is four months, and for other animals it is six months at least. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage establishes the validity of acknowledging a pregnancy (of a slave-girl or an animal) as belonging to someone else. The commentator explains that such an acknowledgment cannot be taken literally (since the child is not owned separately), so it is interpreted as referencing a previous bequest by someone else. The acknowledgment is valid without stating the cause because there is a plausible legal scenario (a bequest). For a slave-girl's pregnancy, the recipient is entitled if the child could have been conceived before the acknowledgment (i.e., born within six months of acknowledgment if the woman has a husband, or within two years if she is in iddah, considering the possibility of delayed birth). For animals, certainty of pregnancy at the time of acknowledgment is required, using expert knowledge of gestation periods (sheep: 4 months; other animals: 6 months).

Key Terms
حمل (ḥaml): Pregnancy; the fetus in the womb
وصية (waṣiyyah): Bequest; the legal interpretation given to acknowledge pregnancy as a bequest from a third party

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Six months
B) One year
C) Two years
D) Four months

Key Takeaway
Acknowledgment of pregnancy is valid only under the interpretative framework of a prior bequest, with specific gestation-based conditions depending on the type of female.

Iqrar for a Fetus: Conditions and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Conditions and validity of iqrar for a fetus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for valid iqrar for a fetus and explain the dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the need for a valid cause of ownership.

Core Idea
Iqrar for a fetus requires either a valid cause (Abu Yusuf) or can be interpreted as one (Muhammad) — always check the cause.

When a person acknowledges a right in favor of an unborn fetus (haml), special rules apply because the fetus lacks legal personality. The Hanafi school explores whether the confessor must specify a valid legal cause (like inheritance or bequest) to justify the acknowledgment, or whether the acknowledgment can stand on its own, interpreted as referring to a plausible cause. This lesson unpacks the conditions and the landmark dispute between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf, showing how the same principle is applied to both human and animal fetuses.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its conditions
Identify that iqrar for a fetus is valid provided the fetus existed at the time of iqrar. Note the two key conditions: (1) existence of the fetus at the time of iqrar (or possibility), and (2) for a human fetus, the woman must give birth within six months if married or two years if divorced; for an animal, the minimum gestation period applies.
These timeframes ensure that the fetus could have been alive when the iqrar was made.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
The central disagreement: Abu Yusuf insists that a valid cause (sabab salih) must be mentioned (e.g., inheritance, bequest), while Muhammad holds that even without a cause, the iqrar is valid because it can be interpreted as referring to a valid cause to uphold the confessor's words.
The text notes that the three other imams (Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) side with Muhammad.
3
Analyze the proofs of each position
Abu Yusuf argues that absolute iqrar defaults to contractual obligations, which do not apply to a fetus. Muhammad argues that a sane person's statements are presumed correct, so the iqrar should be interpreted as referring to a valid cause.
Both aim to balance the sanctity of confession with legal coherence.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In practice, if a man says 'The fetus of this woman owes me 1000 dirhams' without any explanation, Muhammad and the three imams consider it valid, while Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa consider it invalid unless a cause is stated.
Later Hanafi fatwa often relies on Muhammad's view, but the conservative position remains with Abu Yusuf.
5
Apply the rules for existence and distribution
If the child is born alive, the acknowledged property goes to it. If stillborn, the property returns to the original owner's heirs. For twins, they share equally if same gender; if mixed, in bequest they share equally, but in inheritance the male gets twice the female's share.
This demonstrates that the iqrar is conditional on the outcome of the birth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح الإقرار بالحمل بأن قال: حمل جاريتي هذه، أو حمل شاتي هذه لفلان. (عيني)] وإن لم يبين السبب بالإجماع؛ لأن لتصحيحه جهة واحدة، وهي الوصية من جهة غيره بأن يحمل هذا الإقرار على أن رجلاً أوصى بالحمل لرجل، ومات الوصي، والآن يقر وارثه بأنه للموصى له، ثم إن كان يقر به حمل جارية يستحقه المقر له إذا علم وجوده عند الإقرار أو احتمل ذلك بأن تضعه لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الإقرار إن كانت ذات زوج أو لأقل من سنتين إذا كانت معتدة، وإن كان حمل شاة يشترط التيقن بوجوده وقت الإقرار، فيقدر بأدنى مدة يتصور ذلك عند أهل الخبرة على ما جرت به عادتهم، وأقل مدة حمل الشاة أربعة أشهر وأقلها لبقية الدواب ستة أشهر. (عيني، فتح)

وللحمل إن بين سببا صالحا وإلا لا، وإن أقر بشرط الخيار لزمه المال وبطل الشرط.

وللحمل إلخ [وصح الإقرار للحمل بأن قال رجل: الحمل فلانة علي ألف درهم. (معدن)] أي يصح الإقرار أيضاً لحمل امرأة مثلاً بشرط أن يبين المقر سبباً صالحاً للتمليك، مثل أن يقول: مات أبوه فورثه أو أوصى له به فلان، وهذا عند أبي يوسف، وعند محمد: يجوز، وإن لم يبين السبب الصالح؛ لأنه يمكن أن يحمل على الأسباب الصالحة؛ لثبوت الملك تصحيحاً لإقراره وبه قالت الثلاثة، ولأبي يوسف أن مطلق الإقرار ينصرف إلى الواجب بالمعاملات دون غيره، وذلك لا يتصور في الجنين إلا إذا بين سبباً صالحاً، ثم إذا صح الإقرار للحمل إذا جاءت به في مدة يعلم أنه كان موجوداً وقت الإقرار أو يحتمل ذلك بأن تضعه لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الإقرار إذا كانت ذات زوج، أو لأقل من سنتين من وقت الفراق إذا كانت معتدة.
ثم إن ولدته حياً كان له ما أقر به وإن ولدته ميتا يرد إلى ورثة الموصي أو ورثة أبيه، وإن ولدت ولدين، فإن كانا ذكرين أو أنثيين فهو بينهما نصفان وإن كان أحدهما ذكراً والآخر أنثى فكذلك في الوصية، وفي الإرث للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين. [رمز الحقائق: ۲۲۱/۲] (فتح) صالحاً: لثبوت الملك للحمل بأن قال: لحمل فلانة علي كذا من جهة ميراث ورثه من أبيه، أو وصية أوصي بها له. (معدن)
وإلا لا: أي وإن لم يبين سبباً صالحاً، وهو يعم الوجهين: الأول: أن يبين سبباً غير صالح لثبوت الملك مثل ما إذا قال: باعني أو أقرضني أو آجرني هذا الجنين، والثاني: أن يبهم الإقرار ولم يبين سبباً فلا يصح إقراره، بل يلغو في الوجه الأول، وفي الوجه الثاني يصح عند محمد؛ لأن الإقرار إذا صدر من أهله مضافاً إلى محله كان حجة يجب العمل بها، ولا نزاع في صدره من أهله؛ لأنه هو المفروض، وأمكن إضافته إلى محله بحمله على السبب الصالح حملاً لكلام العاقل على الصحة، ولا يصح عند الشيخين، والحاصل أن للمسألة ثلاث صور: إما أن يبهم الإقرار فهو على الخلاف، وإما أن يبين سبباً صالحاً فيجوز بالإجماع، وإما أن يبين سبباً غير صالح فلا يجوز بالإجماع. (عيني، فتح، عناية)
And the iqrar for a fetus is valid if he says: 'the fetus of this slave-girl of mine' or 'the fetus of this ewe of mine belongs to so-and-so.' (Ayni) Even if he did not explain the cause, by consensus? No, the text says 'even if he did not explain the cause' is not correct; rather it is valid by consensus only if there is one possible cause, which is a bequest from someone else: that a man bequeathed the fetus to another, then the executor died, and now his heir acknowledges that it belongs to the legatee. Then if he acknowledges a fetus of a slave-girl, the confesse is entitled to it if he knows it existed at the time of iqrar or if it is possible that it existed, i.e., she gives birth less than six months from the time of iqrar if she has a husband, or less than two years if she is in her waiting period. If it is a fetus of a ewe, certainty of its existence at the time of iqrar is required, and the minimum period for that is estimated by experts according to custom; the minimum pregnancy for a ewe is four months, and for other animals six months. (Ayni, Fath)

And for the fetus, if he explains a valid cause, then yes; otherwise no. And if he acknowledges with a condition of option, the money is binding and the condition is void.

And for the fetus, etc. (Maden) i.e., iqrar for a fetus of a woman is also valid, e.g., if a man says 'the fetus of so-and-so owes me one thousand dirhams,' provided that the confessor explains a valid cause for ownership, such as saying 'his father died and he inherited it' or 'so-and-so bequeathed it to him.' This is according to Abu Yusuf. According to Muhammad, it is valid even if he does not explain the valid cause, because it can be interpreted as a valid cause to confirm ownership, to validate his iqrar. The three (other imams) also hold this view. Abu Yusuf argues that an absolute iqrar applies only to obligations arising from transactions, not others, and that is not conceivable for a fetus unless a valid cause is explained. Then, the iqrar for a fetus is valid if she gives birth within a period that he knows it existed at the time of iqrar or possibly existed, i.e., she gives birth less than six months from the time of iqrar if she has a husband, or less than two years from the time of separation if she is in waiting period.

Then, if she gives birth alive, the child gets what was acknowledged; if stillborn, it is returned to the heirs of the testator or his father. If she gives birth to twins, if both are male or both female, they share equally. If one is male and the other female, it is the same in bequest, but in inheritance the male gets twice the female's share. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/221) (Fath) 'Valid cause': to establish ownership for the fetus, e.g., saying 'the fetus of so-and-so owes me such-and-such from inheritance inherited from his father, or a bequest made to him.' (Maden)

'Otherwise no': i.e., if he does not explain a valid cause. This covers two cases: first, he explains an invalid cause such as 'he sold to me' or 'he lent to me' or 'he leased to me this fetus'; second, he leaves the iqrar ambiguous without specifying a cause. In the first case, his iqrar is invalid and void. In the second case, according to Muhammad it is valid because iqrar when issued by a competent person and directed to a proper subject is a proof that must be acted upon, and it can be interpreted as a valid cause, applying the principle of interpreting a sane person's words as correct. However, it is not valid according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). In summary, there are three situations: either the iqrar is ambiguous (which is disputed), or he explains a valid cause (valid by consensus), or he explains an invalid cause (invalid by consensus). (Ayni, Fath, Inayah)
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of making an iqrar (acknowledgement) in favour of an unborn fetus (haml). The core issue is that since a fetus is not a legal person at the time of iqrar, acknowledging a right for it requires special conditions. The text presents the views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. According to Abu Yusuf, iqrar for a fetus is valid only if the confessor specifies a valid legal cause for the entitlement, such as inheritance or bequest. The reason is that the default interpretation of iqrar is that it refers to a contractual obligation (like sale or loan), which cannot apply to a fetus. By specifying a cause like inheritance, the iqrar becomes plausible. According to Imam Muhammad, however, the iqrar is valid even without specifying a cause, because the confessor's words can be interpreted as meaning a valid cause, in order to uphold the iqrar. The other three imams (Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) agree with Muhammad. The text then discusses the timeframe within which the fetus must be born to establish its existence at the time of iqrar: for a married woman, less than six months; for a divorced woman in her waiting period, less than two years. For animals like ewes, the minimum pregnancy duration is four months. After birth, if the child is born alive, it takes the acknowledged property. If stillborn, the property returns to the original owner's heirs. In case of twins, they share equally if same gender; if mixed, in bequest they share equally, but in inheritance the male gets twice the female's share. The text also clarifies that if the confessor mentions an invalid cause (e.g., 'he sold me this fetus'), the iqrar is invalid by consensus. If the iqrar is ambiguous (no cause mentioned), Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa hold it invalid, while Muhammad holds it valid.

Key Terms
الحمل (al-haml): The unborn fetus, whether human or animal.
السبب الصالح (al-sabab al-salih): A valid legal cause for ownership, such as inheritance, bequest, or gift, that can apply to a fetus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only if the confessor specifies a valid cause
B) Always, even without a cause
C) Only if the fetus is born alive
D) Only if the mother attests

Key Takeaway
Iqrar for a fetus is valid only if the fetus existed at the time, and the cause requirement varies between Hanafi positions — always check the specific conditions.

Acknowledgment for a Fetus: Valid Cause Requirement
Ruling on acknowledgment for a fetus with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on acknowledgment for a fetus and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the requirement of a valid cause.

Core Idea
Acknowledgment for a fetus is valid only if a valid cause is mentioned, according to the relied-upon Hanafi view.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a subtle area of Hanafi fiqh: can one make a valid acknowledgment of debt or property for a fetus? The matn gives a conditional ruling, and the commentary reveals a dispute between two of Imam Abu Hanifa's leading students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the school's methodology, especially the use of interpretation (haml) and the default presumption about acknowledgments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the act under discussion (iqrar for a fetus) and the basic ruling: it is valid with conditions.
The matn says: 'If he mentions a valid cause [it is valid], otherwise not.'
2
Understand the condition of a valid cause
What counts as a valid cause? The commentary specifies inheritance or bequest. Contracts like sale or loan are invalid causes.
A valid cause is one that can establish ownership for the fetus.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf requires explicit mention of a valid cause; Muhammad holds that even without a cause, the acknowledgment can be interpreted validly.
This is the core ikhtilaf in the passage.
4
Analyze the reasoning
Abu Yusuf: absolute iqrar defaults to transactional obligations, which don't apply to a fetus. Muhammad: a rational person's speech should be interpreted validly.
Contrast the two methods of legal reasoning.
5
Apply the relied-upon view
The mufta bihi is Abu Yusuf's view: mention of a valid cause is required for validity.
Fatwa follows this view for caution.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللحمل إن بين سببا صالحا وإلا لا
وللحمل إلخ [وصح الإقرار للحمل بأن قال رجل: الحمل فلانة علي ألف درهم. (معدن)] أي يصح الإقرار أيضاً لحمل امرأة مثلاً بشرط أن يبين المقر سبباً صالحاً للتمليك، مثل أن يقول: مات أبوه فورثه أو أوصى له به فلان، وهذا عند أبي يوسف، وعند محمد: يجوز، وإن لم يبين السبب الصالح؛ لأنه يمكن أن يحمل على الأسباب الصالحة؛ لثبوت الملك تصحيحاً لإقراره وبه قالت الثلاثة، ولأبي يوسف أن مطلق الإقرار ينصرف إلى الواجب بالمعاملات دون غيره، وذلك لا يتصور في الجنين إلا إذا بين سبباً صالحاً، ثم إذا صح الإقرار للحمل إذا جاءت به في مدة يعلم أنه كان موجوداً وقت الإقرار أو يحتمل ذلك بأن تضعه لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الإقرار إذا كانت ذات زوج، أو لأقل من سنتين من وقت الفراق إذا كانت معتدة. 
ثم إن ولدته حياً كان له ما أقر به وإن ولدته ميتا يرد إلى ورثة الموصي أو ورثة أبيه، وإن ولدت ولدين، فإن كانا ذكرين أو أنثيين فهو بينهما نصفان وإن كان أحدهما ذكراً والآخر أنثى فكذلك في الوصية، وفي الإرث للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين. [رمز الحقائق: ۲۲۱/۲] (فتح) صالحاً: لثبوت الملك للحمل بأن قال: لحمل فلانة علي كذا من جهة ميراث ورثه من أبيه، أو وصية أوصي بها له. (معدن)
وإلا لا: أي وإن لم يبين سبباً صالحاً، وهو يعم الوجهين: الأول: أن يبين سبباً غير صالح لثبوت الملك مثل ما إذا قال: باعني أو أقرضني أو آجرني هذا الجنين، والثاني: أن يبهم الإقرار ولم يبين سبباً فلا يصح إقراره، بل يلغو في الوجه الأول، وفي الوجه الثاني يصح عند محمد؛ لأن الإقرار إذا صدر من أهله مضافاً إلى محله كان حجة يجب العمل بها، ولا نزاع في صدره من أهله؛ لأنه هو المفروض، وأمكن إضافته إلى محله بحمله على السبب الصالح حملاً لكلام العاقل على الصحة، ولا يصح عند الشيخين، والحاصل أن للمسألة ثلاث صور: إما أن يبهم الإقرار فهو على الخلاف، وإما أن يبين سبباً صالحاً فيجوز بالإجماع، وإما أن يبين سبباً غير صالح فلا يجوز بالإجماع. (عيني، فتح، عناية)
And for the fetus: if he mentions a valid cause [it is valid], otherwise not. [Commentary: It is valid to make an acknowledgment for the fetus, as when a man says: 'The fetus of so-and-so owes me a thousand dirhams.' Meaning: it is also valid to acknowledge for a pregnant woman's fetus, on condition that the one making the acknowledgment mentions a valid cause for ownership, such as saying: 'His father died and he inherited from him' or 'So-and-so bequeathed to him.' This is according to Abu Yusuf. According to Muhammad, it is permissible even if he does not mention the valid cause, because it is possible to interpret it as a valid cause, in order to affirm ownership and validate his acknowledgment. The three (the two companions and Imam Muhammad?) said that. And for Abu Yusuf, an absolute acknowledgment is restricted to obligations arising from transactions, and that cannot be imagined for a fetus unless a valid cause is mentioned. Then, the acknowledgment for the fetus is valid if she gives birth within a time indicating that the fetus existed at the time of the acknowledgment or could have existed, i.e., if she gives birth less than six months from the acknowledgment if she is married, or less than two years from separation if she is in her waiting period ('iddah). Then, if she gives birth to a live child, what was acknowledged belongs to it; if born dead, it is returned to the testator's heirs or the father's heirs. If she gives birth to twins, if both are male or both female, it is shared equally; if one is male and the other female, likewise in bequest; but in inheritance, the male gets double the female's share. [Sign: Ramz al-Haqa'iq 221/2] (Fath) 'Valid cause': to affirm ownership for the fetus by saying: 'The fetus of so-and-so owes me such-and-such from inheritance from his father or a bequest made to him.' (Ma'din) 'Otherwise not': i.e., if he does not mention a valid cause, this includes two cases: first, if he mentions a cause not valid for ownership, like saying: 'This fetus sold to me, or lent to me, or hired to me'; second, if he leaves it ambiguous and mentions no cause. In the first case, the acknowledgment is invalid and void; in the second case, it is valid according to Muhammad because an acknowledgment coming from a competent person and attributed to its proper subject is a proof that must be acted upon, and there is no dispute about its issuing from a competent person (as that is assumed), and it is possible to attribute it to its subject by interpreting it as a valid cause, interpreting a rational person's speech as correct. It is not valid according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). Summary: there are three scenarios: if he leaves it ambiguous, there is disagreement; if he mentions a valid cause, it is permitted by consensus; if he mentions an invalid cause, it is not permitted by consensus. (Ayni, Fath, Inayah)
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on making an acknowledgment (iqrar) for a fetus (haml) in Hanafi fiqh. The matn states: 'If he mentions a valid cause [it is valid], otherwise not.' The commentary expands on this. The key dispute is between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Abu Yusuf requires that the person making the acknowledgment explicitly mention a valid cause for the fetus's ownership, such as inheritance or bequest, because an absolute acknowledgment is understood as arising from transactions, which is not possible for a fetus without a cause. Muhammad holds that even without mentioning a cause, the acknowledgment can be valid, because the court can interpret the acknowledgment in a way that is valid to uphold its validity (haml al-kalam 'ala al-sihha). The commentary then sets conditions: the fetus must have existed at the time of acknowledgment (within six months if married, two years if in 'iddah). It also addresses distribution: if the child is born alive, it receives the amount; if stillborn, the amount goes back; if twins, equal division regardless of gender; but in inheritance the male gets twice the female. The commentary further categorizes three scenarios: ambiguous (disagreement), valid cause (consensus), invalid cause (consensus). This is a classic example of intra-Hanafi ijtihad and the importance of proper legal reasoning (qiyas and istihsan).

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): A legal acknowledgment of a right or debt owed to another; a unilateral declaration that creates liability.
حمل (haml): A fetus in the womb, considered a legal entity with potential ownership rights.
سبب صالح (sabun salih): A valid legal cause for ownership, such as inheritance, bequest, or legitimate contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The name of the fetus
B) A valid cause for ownership such as inheritance or bequest
C) The exact value of the debt
D) The time of payment

Key Takeaway
Acknowledgment for a fetus is valid only with a valid cause (inheritance/bequest) according to the relied-upon Hanafi view, though Imam Muhammad permits it without explicit cause.

The Hanafi View on Istithna' in Acknowledgments: Modification vs. Extraction
Ruling on exceptions in acknowledgments; Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute over legal nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school treats istithna' as a modification of the acknowledgment and apply this to resolve ambiguous exceptions.

Core Idea
Istithna' changes the meaning of the acknowledgment; it is not an extraction but a statement of the remainder.

In Hanafi fiqh, when someone acknowledges a debt with an exception, the exception is part of the statement, not an opposing extraction. This contrasts with the Shafi'i view and leads to different practical outcomes. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the key condition of continuity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
Recognize that the passage deals with the validity and nature of istithna' (exception) in acknowledgments. The central dispute is whether istithna' is a modification (Hanafi) or an extraction (Shafi'i).
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that istithna' is speaking of the remainder. For example, 'a thousand except a hundred' means nine hundred. The exception is not a negation but part of the intended meaning.
This is based on the linguistic root thanya meaning 'to turn away'.
3
State the Shafi'i position
Al-Shafi'i argues that the initial part of the statement (the full amount) is established, and the exception negates part of it, leading to a conflict that cancels to the extent of the excepted amount.
4
Follow each proof
Hanafi proof: analogy with divorce and manumission, where istithna' is accepted; if it were extraction, it would imply revocation, which is impossible after occurrence. Shafi'i proof: literal reading of conflict between affirmation and negation.
5
Determine the thamarah
The practical difference appears in ambiguous exceptions. For 'a thousand except a hundred or fifty,' Hanafi says nine hundred (least amount), Shafi'i says nine hundred and fifty.
Hanafi relies on the principle that liability is free, so doubt resolves in favor of the debtor.

(معدن) والمراد بالاتصال بحسب التلفظ بحيث لا يسكت بين المستثنى والمستثنى منه لا الاستثناء المتصل، والسكوت لنفس أو سعال، أو أخذ فم، أو النداء بينهما لا يضر كقوله لك علي ألف درهم يا فلان إلا عشرة، وقوله : لزمه الباقي بعد الاستثناء" سواء كان المستثنى أقل مما بقي أو أكثر، وعن أبي يوسف وهو قول مالك وأحمد والفراء: لا يصح الاستثناء إلا إذا كان الباقي أكثر ؛ لأن العرب لم تتكلم به.
قلنا: تكلمت به العرب، وهو موجود في القرآن، قال تعالى: قم اللَّيْلَ إِلَّا قَلِيلاً نِصْفَهُ أَوِ انْقُصْ مِنْهُ قَلِيلًا أَوْ زِدْ عَلَيْهِ (المزمل: ٤،٣،٢) وقال تعالى: ﴿لَأُغْوِيَنَّهُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ إِلَّا عِبَادَكَ مِنْهُمُ الْمُخْلَصِينَ ﴾ (ص: ۸۳٬۸۲) ثم قال: ﴿وَإِنَّ عِبَادِي لَيْسَ لَكَ عَلَيْهِمْ سُلْطَانُ إِلَّا مَنِ اتَّبَعَكَ مِنَ الْغَاوِينَ) (الحجر: ٤٢) فاستثنى المخلصين تارة والغاوين أخرى، فأيهما كان أكثر، فالحجة قائمة، وإنما يشترط الاتصال؛ لأن الكلام لا يتم إلا بآخره، فإذا انقطع الكلام فقد تم، فلا يعتبر الاستثناء بعده، وعلى هذا أجمع العلماء إلا ابن عباس الهما، فإن عنده يصح الاستثناء وإن كان مفصولاً؛ لقوله عليه: "لأغزون قريشاً " ثم قال: "بعد سنة إن شاء الله"، فعلم أنه صح مفصولاً، قلنا: هو مغير، والمغير لا يصح .
Chapter on exception (istithna') and what is in its meaning. It is valid to except some of what one has acknowledged. The chapter on exception: [It is a request for 'thunya,' which is turning away, i.e., change and extraction. In the terminology of jurists: it is speaking of the remainder after the exception. (Ayni)] Since he mentioned the consequence of acknowledgment without change, he now begins the consequence with a changer, which is exception and what is in its meaning, as a changer like a condition. 'Istithna'' is derived from 'thanya,' which means 'turning away' linguistically; one says 'thanaytuhu' meaning I turned him away from his need. And it is speaking of the remainder according to us in meaning, not in form. According to al-Shafi'i: it is an extraction by way of opposition. So when a man says: 'I owe a thousand dirhams to so-and-so except a hundred,' its interpretation according to us is: 'I owe so-and-so nine hundred' and he did not speak of the thousand with respect to the obligation of the hundred. According to al-Shafi'i, its interpretation is: 'I owe a thousand except a hundred, and those hundred are not owed by me.' The initial part of the statement imposes it, and the exception negates it, so they conflict and cancel each other to the extent of the excepted amount. His madhhab is problematic, because exception is permissible in divorce and manumission, and if it were extraction, it would not be allowed since they do not admit of revocation and lifting after occurrence. The fruit of the difference appears when he says: 'I owe him a thousand dirhams except a hundred or fifty.' According to us, he is liable for nine hundred; because since he spoke of the remainder and it prevents entry, we doubt whether he spoke of the additional, and the principle is that the liability is free, so he is not liable for the extra due to doubt. So it becomes similar to when he says: 'I owe nine hundred or nine hundred and fifty,' in which case he is liable for the lesser amount. According to al-Shafi'i, since the entire thousand entered, doubt arose about the exclusion, so the lesser, i.e., fifty, is excluded, and the rest remains, so he is liable for nine hundred and fifty. And what is in its meaning: such as saying: 'I owe the thousand as the price of a slave I have not received,' for his saying 'I have not received' is in the meaning of an exception. [Sign: Ramz al-Haqa'iq 221/2] (Fath, Inayah). 'Valid; it is valid to except some etc.' [Whether the part is less than the remainder or more. (Ma'din). And what is meant by 'conjunction' is according to utterance such that there is no pause between the excepted and the exceptor, not the continuous excepted. Pausing for breath, coughing, clearing the mouth, or an interjection does not harm, e.g., saying: 'I owe you a thousand dirhams, O so-and-so, except ten.' And it is said: 'He is liable for the remainder after the exception,' whether the excepted is less than the remainder or more. From Abu Yusuf, and it is the saying of Malik, Ahmad, and al-Farra': the exception is only valid if the remainder is more, because the Arabs did not use it otherwise. We say: The Arabs did use it, and it exists in the Quran. Allah said: 'Stand the night except a little—half of it or subtract from it a little or add to it' (73:2-4). And Allah said: 'I will surely mislead them all except Your chosen servants from among them' (38:82-83). Then He said: 'And indeed, My servants have no authority over them except those who follow you from the misled ones' (15:42). So He excepted the chosen ones at one time and the misled ones at another, and whichever is more, the proof stands. What is required is conjunction, because speech is not complete except with its end; if the speech is cut off, it is complete, and the exception after it is not considered. Upon this the scholars are unanimous, except Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, for according to him, exception is valid even if separated, based on his statement: 'I will certainly invade the Quraish' then he said: 'a year later, if Allah wills.' So it became known that it is valid separated. We say: That is a changer, and a changer is not valid.]
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on exceptions (istithna') in acknowledgments. The matn states that it is valid to except part of what one has acknowledged. The commentary first defines istithna' linguistically: it comes from thanya, meaning to turn or exclude. Technically, it means speaking of the remainder after the exception. The author then distinguishes between two views: The Hanafi view, that the intended meaning of 'a thousand except a hundred' is actually nine hundred, meaning the exception is part of the statement, not an oppositional extraction. Al-Shafi'i holds the view that the entire thousand is initially acknowledged, then the exception extracts part, leading to a conflict that requires balancing. The practical difference (thamaratu al-khilaf) appears when the amount is ambiguous: e.g., 'I owe a thousand except a hundred or fifty.' Under the Hanafi view, the debtor only owes nine hundred, because the original statement is interpreted as specifying the remainder, and doubt resolves in favor of the debtor (original freedom of liability). Under Shafi'i view, the debtor owes nine hundred and fifty, because the thousand is established, then the lesser exception removes fifty only. The Hanafi view is supported by analogies with divorce and manumission, where exception is common and cannot be revocation. The passage also discusses the condition of continuity (ittisal): the exception must be uttered continuously without a significant pause. The Quranic examples are provided to counter Abu Yusuf's (and others') view that the remainder must be greater than the excepted part; the examples show both cases. The final part refutes Ibn Abbas's view that exception can be separated, noting that exception is a changer and cannot apply after speech is complete.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna'): A linguistic exception that modifies the scope of the acknowledgment; it is part of the statement, not an independent negation.
ثنيا (thunya): The act of exception; the excluded part.
اتصال (ittisal): The condition that the exception be uttered immediately after the main statement without a significant pause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the practical difference (thamaratu al-khilaf) between the Hanafi and Shafi'i views on istithna' when someone says: 'I owe a thousand dirhams except a hundred or fifty'?
A) Under both views, he owes nine hundred and fifty
B) Under Hanafi, he owes nine hundred; under Shafi'i, nine hundred and fifty
C) Under Hanafi, he owes nine hundred and fifty; under Shafi'i, nine hundred
D) Under both views, he owes nine hundred

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school views istithna' as a modification that specifies the remainder, leading to the debtor owing the least amount in case of ambiguity.

Connected Exception and Invalidity of Exception of the Whole in Iqrār
Ruling on connected exception and invalidity of exception of the whole

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid exception in iqrār and identify when an exception constitutes a retraction.

Core Idea
A connected exception is valid; an exception of the whole is a retraction and void.

In Hanafi fiqh, when a person acknowledges a debt or ownership, they may attach an exception excluding part of what they acknowledged. However, the exception must leave a remainder; otherwise it is considered a retraction of the entire acknowledgement, which is invalid. This lesson explains the ruling on connected exceptions and the prohibition of exceptions that consume the whole subject, supported by Quranic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is making an exception (istithnā') in an acknowledgment (iqrār). Determine the ruling for two types: connected exception and exception of the whole.
Connected exception leaves a remainder; exception of the whole leaves none.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof cited is the Prophet's practice of saying 'in shā' Allāh' in obedience to Quranic commands (18:23–24). This establishes the validity of connected exception.
The verses command mentioning Allah's will when speaking about future actions.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah
The Prophet's exception is a form of istithnā' that leaves the main statement intact. By analogy, any connected exception in iqrār that leaves a remainder is valid. Exception of the whole is invalid because it leaves no remainder - it nullifies the acknowledgment.
The key is the possibility of a remainder after the exception.
4
Note the ikhtilāf
The passage does not mention opposing views, but the ruling is consistent with the Hanafi school. Other schools may have different conditions for exception validity.
This is a mukhtalaf fih issue, but the text presents only the Hanafi position.
5
Apply the ruling
If someone says 'I owe you ten dirhams except ten', the exception consumes the whole, so it is a retraction and the full ten dirhams are owed. If they say 'except one', the exception is valid and nine are owed.
The exception must be muttasil (connected) to the acknowledgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متصلا، ولزمه زمه الباقي، لا استثناء الكل، وصح استثناء الكيلي والوزني من الدراهم لا غيرهما المقر بعد الاستثناء إلا متصلاً كالشرط واستثناء النبي عليه كان امتثالاً لأمره تعالى، وهو قوله: ﴿وَاذْكُرْ رَبَّكَ إِذَا نَسِيتَ (الكهف: ٢٤)، وقوله: ﴿وَلا تَقُولَنَّ لِشَيْءٍ إِنِّي فَاعِلٌ ذَلِكَ غَداً إِلَّا أَنْ يَشَاءَ اللَّهُ ﴾ (الكهف:٢٤،٢٣) (عيني، فتح، كفاية) متصلا أي حال كون الاستثناء متصلاً بالإقرار (ط) (معدن لا استثناء الكل: أي لا يصح استثناء الكل؛ لما ذكرنا أن الاستثناء تكلم بالحاصل بعد الثنيا، ولا حاصل بعد الكل، فيكون رجوعاً، والرجوع عن الإقرار باطل، موصولاً كان أو مفصولاً، وهذا إذا كان الاستثناء بعين لفظه وكذا إذا كان بمرادفه؛ لعدم ما يتكلم بعده، فكان رجوعاً كقوله : له علي عشرة إلا عشرة أو له علي مائة إلا خمسين وخمسين. أما إذا كان لفظ المستثنى بخلاف لفظ المستثنى منه نحو عبيدي أحرار إلا هؤلاء، أو نسائي طوالق إلا هؤلاء، أو عبيدي أحرار إلا مباركاً وسالماً وربيعاً، أو نسائي طوالق إلا زينب وعمرة وفاطمة، وليس له عبيد ولا نساء غير المستثنى صح الاستثناء، فلا يعتق واحد منهم، ولا تطلق واحدة منهن؛ لأنه إذا اختلف اللفظ يتوهم بقاء الشيء من المستثنى منه؛ إذ اللفظ صالح، ولا يشترط حقيقة البقاء؛ لأن الاستثناء يتبع صحة الكلام لفظاً، وذلك يكفي لصحة الاستثناء، فبالنظر إلى ذات اللفظ أمكن أن يجعل المستثنى بعد ما يتناول الصدر، والامتناع من خارج، بخلاف ما إذا كان بعين ذلك اللفظ، فإنه لا يمكن جعله تكلماً بالحاصل بعد الاستثناء. (عيني، فتح)
Connected, and what remains is binding upon him, and exception of the whole is not valid. Exception of measured and weighed items from dirhams is valid, not others. The one making acknowledgment after the exception [must be] connected, like a condition. The Prophet's exception was in obedience to Allah's command: [Quran 18:24] and [Quran 18:23-24]. 'Connected' means the exception is connected to the acknowledgment. Exception of the whole is invalid because there is no remainder after the whole, so it is a retraction, and retraction of acknowledgment is invalid, whether connected or disconnected. This applies when the exception uses the same wording or its synonym. For example, saying 'I owe ten except ten' or 'I owe one hundred except fifty and fifty'. However, if the wording of the excepted item differs from the excepted-from, like 'my slaves are free except these' or 'my wives are divorced except these', and he has no other slaves or wives, the exception is valid, and none of them are freed or divorced. This is because when the wording differs, the possibility remains that some are excepted from the excepted-from, since the wording can accommodate it. Actual remaining is not required because exception depends on the verbal validity of the speech, and that suffices for its validity. From the perspective of the wording itself, it is possible to make the excepted item apply to what the first part encompasses, with the prevention from outside, unlike when the same wording is used, where it cannot be considered speech about the remainder after the exception.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq, with commentary from al-`Ayni and Fath al-Qadir, states the ruling on connected exception in acknowledgment. The rule: the exception must be connected (muttasil) to the acknowledgment. If it is disconnected, it is a retraction, which is invalid. Exception of the whole (istithna' al-kull) is specifically invalid because it leaves nothing, effectively nullifying the acknowledgment. The proof given is the Prophet's practice of saying 'in sha' Allah' in obedience to Quranic commands. The passage then distinguishes between cases where the excepted item uses the same wording as the excepted-from (e.g., 'ten dirhams except ten') — which is invalid as it is a retraction — and cases where the wording differs (e.g., 'my slaves are free except these'). In the latter, the exception is valid provided the person has no other slaves or wives beyond those excepted. The rationale is that differing wording allows the possibility that the exception leaves some part, even if not actual, because the wording can bear that meaning. This is a Hanafi position considering the intendant of speech, not just its literal effect.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithnā'): Excluding part of the confessed amount from the acknowledgment using an exceptive phrase.
متصلا (muttasil): Connected exception uttered in the same speech without interruption.
إقرار (iqrār): Legal acknowledgment of a right or debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is exception of the whole (istithnā' al-kull) in an acknowledgment invalid according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) It is considered a retraction because no remainder is left after the exception.
B) It is invalid only if the wording is different from the excepted-from.
C) It is invalid because it violates the condition of connection.
D) It is actually valid if the exception is made in a disconnected manner.

Key Takeaway
A connected exception in iqrār is valid only if it leaves a possible remainder; otherwise it is an invalid retraction.

Exception of Measured and Weighed Items from Dirhams and Dinars
Dispute on validity of excepting different-genus measured items from dirhams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on excepting measured/weighed items from dirhams/dinars and evaluate the opposing arguments from Imam Muhammad, Shafi'i, and Malik.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school permits excepting measured/weighed items from dirhams because they share the attribute of being a price (thamaniyya), even if the genus differs.

This lesson examines a classic dispute within the Hanafi school and beyond: can a person acknowledge a debt of dirhams and then except a different item like wheat? The majority of the school, led by Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, allow it by juristic preference (istihsan), while Imam Muhammad insists on strict qiyas. The dispute turns on whether the excepted item shares the essential attribute of being a price. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying rulings on acknowledgments.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): excepting measured (kayli) or weighed (wazni) items from dirhams/dinars is valid by istihsan. Also note the exception applies to countable items like coins and walnuts.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of the two shaykhs.
2
Identify the opposing view and its proof
Imam Muhammad holds that such an exception is invalid by qiyas because the excepted item is from a different genus. He draws an analogy with excepting a garment or sheep, which is invalid. Zufar and Ahmad also hold this view.
The opposing dalil is qiyas: exception of a different genus is invalid.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response (istihsan)
The two shaykhs argue that measured/weighed items are of the same genus in meaning (thaman) as dirhams because both can be borrowed, deferred, and serve as a standard of value. Istihsan overrides qiyas based on this meaningful homogeneity.
The key concept is thamaniyya (price-ness).
4
Compare with Shafi'i/Maliki position
Shafi'i and Malik also consider the exception valid, but for a different reason: they hold that unity of genus exists from the perspective of monetary value. The Hanafi response emphasizes linguistic and legal precision: dirhams only include what shares the specific attribute of being a price, not all valuable items.
This shows a difference in the definition of genus unity.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If someone says 'I owe 1000 dirhams except a qafiz of wheat,' Hanafi (two shaykhs): valid, he owes 1000 minus the value of wheat. Muhammad: invalid, he owes full 1000. Shafi'i/Maliki: valid, same result as Hanafi but for different reason.
For non-price items like a sheep, all agree the exception is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكيلي والوزني إلخ وكذا المعدود المتقارب كالفلوس والجوز. (ط)] نحو: ما إذا قال: له علي ألف درهم إلا قفيز حنطة أو إلا ديناراً، وكذا لو قال علي مائة دينار إلا قفيز شعير أو إلا عشرة درهم، صح الإقرار عند الشيخين استحساناً، والقياس: أن لا الاستثناء وهو قول محمد؛ لأنه استثنى من خلاف الجنس، فصار يصح كما لو قال: له علي مائة درهم إلا ثوباً أو إلا شاة، وبه قال زفر وأحمد، ولهما: أنه استثنى مقداراً من مقدرات، وهو من جنسه معنى من حيث أنه يثبت في الذمة حالاً ومؤجلاً، ويجوز استقراضهما وإن اختلفت صورتهما، فإذا كانت في المعنى جنساً واحداً جاز استثناؤها باعتبار المعنى؛ لأن الاستثناء استخراج بطريق المعنى على أن الكلام به عبارة عما وراء المستثنى، ففي المثال المذكور أولاً لزمه ألف درهم إلا قيمة القفيز أو قيمة الدينار، بخلاف الشاة؛ لأنها من ذوات القيم فلا : يصح استثناؤها من الدراهم. الدراهم بأن قال له علي ألف درهم إلا قفيز حنطة أو إلا ديناراً. (عيني) لا غيرهما: أي لا من غير الكيلي والوزني من الدراهم والدنانير كما إذا قال له علي مائة درهم إلا ثوباً أو إلا شاة؛ لعدم وصف الثمنية ،ولو معنى وقال الشافعي: يصح فيطرح عنه قدر قيمة المستثنى؛ لأن الشرط اتحاد الجنس، وهو موجود من حيث المالية، فانتفى المانع بعد تحقق المقتضى وهو التصرف اللفظي، وبه قال مالك، وللشيخين: أن عدم تناول الدراهم غيرها الفظاً لا يرتاب فيه أحد، وإنما الكلام في تناولها إياه حكماً فقلنا: يتناول ما كان على أخص - أوصافها الذي هو الثمنية وهو الدنانير والمقدرات والعددي المتقارب، أما الثوب ونحوه كالشاة فليس بثمن أصلاً، وما ليس بثمن لا يصلح مقدراً للدراهم؛ لعدم المجانسة، فبقي الاستثناء من الدراهم مجهولاً، وجهالة المستثنى يوجب جهالة المستثنى منه، فلا يصح الاستثناء. رمز الحقائق: ٢٢٢/٢]
Measured and weighed items, and similarly countable items that are close like coins and walnuts. For example: if someone says, 'He has one thousand dirhams upon me except a qafiz of wheat or a dinar', or 'I owe one hundred dinars except a qafiz of barley or ten dirhams', the acknowledgment is valid according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) by way of istihsan. The analogy (qiyas) would be that the exception is not valid, which is the opinion of Muhammad, because he made an exception from a different genus, so it would become valid as if he said 'I owe one hundred dirhams except a garment or a sheep.' Zufar and Ahmad agree with Muhammad. Their argument is that he excepted a quantity from quantified items, which is of the same genus in meaning because both are established in the debt both immediately and deferred, and it is permissible to borrow them. So if they are one genus in meaning, exception is valid based on meaning, because exception is extraction by way of meaning, and the speech with it expresses what is outside the excepted item. In the first example, he owes one thousand dirhams except the value of the qafiz or dinar, unlike a sheep because it is of valuable goods and not a fair measure for dirhams. Dirhams: if he says, 'I owe one thousand dirhams except a qafiz of wheat or a dinar.' Not others: meaning not from other than measured and weighed items from dirhams and dinars, as if he says 'I owe one hundred dirhams except a garment or a sheep', due to the lack of the attribute of being a price (thaman). Imam al-Shafi'i said: It is valid, and the value of the excepted item is deducted, because the condition is unity of genus, which exists from the perspective of monetary value, so the obstacle is removed after the existence of the effective cause, which is the verbal disposition. Malik agreed. For the two shaykhs, the fact that dirhams do not include others linguistically is indisputable; the discussion is about their inclusion in legal effect. We say: they include what has the attribute of being a price, which is dinars, measured items, and countable items. As for clothes and sheep, they are not prices at all, and what is not a price cannot be a measure for dirhams due to lack of homogeneity, so the exception from dirhams remains unknown, and uncertainty of the excepted item leads to uncertainty of what is excepted from, so the exception is invalid.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed Hanafi ruling on excepting items of a different genus from dirhams or dinars in an acknowledgment. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): it is valid by istihsan (juristic preference) to except measured (kayli) or weighed (ważni) items, even if they are different in species (e.g., wheat from dirhams, barley from dinars), because they share the attribute of being a price (thaman) in terms of being a standard measure of value. Imam Muhammad disagrees, holding it invalid by qiyas, and is supported by Zufar and Ahmad. The passage then describes the inter-madhhab dispute: Imam al-Shafi'i and Malik consider it valid on the basis that both items have monetary value, so the genus is unified. The Hanafi response is that only items that are themselves prices (like dinars, dirhams, and measured/weighed commodities) can be excepted from dirhams because the exception must be from the same genus in meaning. Items like clothes or animals are not prices, so excepting them leaves the exception vague, invalidating it.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsān): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas due to a stronger evidence or equity.
مقدرات (muqaddarāt): Quantified items that can be measured by volume (kayl) or weight (wazn).
ثمنية (thamaniyya): The attribute of being a medium of exchange or measure of value (price).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the ruling if someone says: 'I owe one thousand dirhams except a qafiz of wheat'?
A) The acknowledgment is invalid, so nothing is owed.
B) The exception is valid, and he owes the dirhams minus the value of the wheat.
C) The exception is invalid, so he owes the full one thousand dirhams.
D) The exception is valid only if the wheat is considered a price.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits excepting measured/weighed items from dirhams because they share the attribute of being a price, even if they differ in species, while non-price items like clothes invalidate the exception.
